          INTRODUCTION
                    Thank you for choosing Kaplan for your educational needs and welcome to the Series 79
                    License Exam Manual (LEM). This manual follows adult learning principles to give you the
                    tools you’ll need to pass your exam on the first attempt.

                    Some of these special features include
                     exam-focused questions and content to maximize exam preparation,
                     an interactive design that integrates content with questions to increase retention, and
                     integrated SecuritiesProTM QBank exam preparation tools to sharpen test-taking skills.


                    Why Do I Need to Pass the Series 79 Exam?
                    The Financial Industry Regulatory Authority (FINRA) or another self-regulatory organization
                    (SRO) requires its members and employees of its members to pass a qualification exam to
                    become registered as an investment banking representative. You must pass the Series 79
                    exam to be qualified to advise on or facilitate debt or equity underwritings, mergers and
                    acquisitions, tender offers, asset sales, or other corporate reorganizations or combinations.


                    Are There Any Prerequisites?
                    There are no prerequisite exams to pass before taking the Series 79 exam; however, in order
                    for a Series 79 registration to become effective, you must pass both the Series 79 qualification
                    exam and the Securities Industry Essentials (SIE) exam.


                    What Is the Series 9 Exam Like?
                    The Series 79 is a 2½-hour, 85-question exam (75 questions scored) administered by FINRA.
                    It is offered as a computer-based exam at Prometric testing centers around the country.


                    What Score Must I Achieve to Pass?
                    You need a score of at least 73% on the Series 79 exam to pass and become eligible for
                    registration as a general securities representative.


                    What Topics Will I See on the Exam?
                    The questions you will see on the Series 79 exam do not appear in any particular order.
                    The computer is programmed to select a new, random set of questions for each exam taker
                    according to the preset topic weighting of the exam. Each Series 79 candidate will see the




          vi

                       same number of questions on each topic but will see a different mix of questions. The
                       Series 79 exam is divided into three critical function areas:

                       FINRA Major Job Function Title                                     # of Questions      % of Exam
                       1. Collection, analysis, and evaluation of data                           37              49%
                       2. Underwriting/new financing transactions, types of offerings,
                                                                                                 20              27%
                          and registration of securities
                       3. Mergers and acquisitions, tender offers, and financial
                                                                                                 18              24%
                          restructuring transactions

                       When you complete your exam, you will receive a printout that reports your performance in
                       each area.


            PREPARING FOR THE EXAM

                       How Is the LEM Organized?
                       The LEM is organized in units. There is a glossary that can help clarify unfamiliar terms
                       you come across, and an index makes it easy to locate content within the LEM. In addition
                       to the regular text, each unit ends with a test. Look for unique features created to help you
                       understand and comprehend the material. When additional emphasis is critical to your
                       success, the following distinctions are made.

                                 TAKE NOTE
                                 Each Take Note provides special information designed to amplify important points.

                                 TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                 Each Test Topic Alert reviews content that is especially likely to appear on the exam.

                                 EXAMPLE
                                 Each Example provides practical applications that convert theory into understanding.


                                 KNOWLEDGE CHECK
                                 Knowledge Checks are a quick interactive review of what you just read. These ensure
                                 you understand and retain the material.



                       Additional Study Resources
                       To accompany and supplement your LEM, your study package may contain additional study
                       resources. Be sure to spend some time on your dashboard, view the best practices video, and
                       understand all that is available to help you study.


                       SecuritiesPro™ QBank
                       Coordinating with the LEM, the SecuritiesProTM QBank includes a large number of questions
                       that are similar in style and content to those you will encounter on the exam. You may use
                       it to generate tests by a specific unit or combination of units. The QBank also allows you




S79_LEM_5E.indb 7                                                                                                         9/1/2020 1:45:48 PM
           viii        Introduction



                                      to create simulated exams that mimic your test. There is no limit on the number of QBank
                                      exams you can create.

                                      One thing you should know about the SecuritiesProTM QBank is that the answer choices are
                                      scrambled each time you take a test. That is, if the first time you saw a specific question, and
                                      the correct answer was choice A, that statement might be choice D the next time. Please keep
                                      this in mind if you need to contact us regarding that question.


                                      Practice and Mastery Exams
                                      You may also have fixed practice and mastery exams. These exams are designed to closely
                                      replicate the true exam experience, both in terms of the degree of difficulty and topical
                                      coverage. They provide a score and diagnostic feedback, but you will not be given access to,
                                      or be able to obtain from Kaplan, correct answers or question explanations. The practice and
                                      mastery exams are sound indicators of potential actual exam scores—the better you do on
                                      these exams, the more likely you are to pass your actual exam. These exams may be taken just
                                      once.


                                      Exam Tips and Content Updates
                                      Don’t forget to monitor your Exam Tips and Content Updates (located on your homepage
                                      or dashboard). When rules and regulations change or we want to share new information
                                      regarding your exam, that information will be posted there.

                                      In addition, try as we may, in a text this large, errors are difficult to avoid. When we become
                                      aware of them, we acknowledge them in the Corrections tab, also located on your dashboard.


                                      What Topics Are Covered in the Course?
                                      The LEM consists of three parts, each devoted to a particular area of study that you will need
                                      to know to pass the Series 79. Each part is divided into units devoted to more specific areas
                                      with which you need to become familiar.

                                      The Series 79 LEM addresses the following topics:

                                       Unit   Topic
                                        1     Company structures
                                        2     Company comparisons
                                        3     Company valuations
                                        4     Capital budgeting
                                        5     Issuing securities

                                        7     The registration process
                                        8     Mergers and acquisitions—sell side
                                        9     Mergers and acquisitions—buy side
                                        10    SEC rules applicable to mergers and acquisitions
                                        11    Tender offers and issuer repurchases
                                        12    Distressed companies

                    How Much Time Should I Spend Studying?
                    Plan to spend approximately 45–55 hours reading the material and carefully answering the
                    questions. Spread your study time over the two to three weeks before the date on which
                    you are scheduled to take the Series 79 exam. Your actual time may vary depending on your
                    reading rate, comprehension, professional background, and study environment.


                    What Is the Best Way to Structure My Study Time?
                    The following schedule is suggested to help you obtain maximum retention from your study
                    efforts. Remember, this is a guideline only, because each individual may require more or less
                    time to complete the steps included.
                    Step 1: Read a Lesson and complete the Knowledge Check. Review rationales for all
                            questions whether you answered them right or wrong. At the end of each unit,
                            there will be a Checkpoint Exam covering all the content contained within that
                            unit. Review the rationales before moving to the next unit.
                    Step 2: On the SecuritiesProTM QBank, create and complete a test for each topic included
                            under that unit heading. For best results, select the maximum number of questions
                            within each topic. Carefully review all rationales. Do an additional test on any
                            topic on which you score under 60%.
                             Do not become too overwhelmed or bogged down in any one unit. You don’t
                               want to lose sight of the finish line because you’re having trouble with one hurdle.
                               Keep moving forward. It’s a steady pace that wins the race.
                             View rationales after each question initially, and spend time studying each
                               rationale to learn the concepts. Later, you will want to create exam scenarios in
                               which scores and rationales are viewed at the end of each exam.
                             Perfection is not the goal during the reading phase; while scores in the mid- to
                               high-60s are good initially, aiming for at least 73% is best.
                    Step 3: When you have completed all the units and their Knowledge Checks in the LEM,
                            using the Securities Pro QBank, concentrate on comprehensive exams covering all
                            the material. With your comprehensive testing, it is best to view correct answers
                            and rationales only after the test is completed. Plan to spend at least one week
                            practicing before your scheduled test date (10–20 hours).
                             You should complete at least eight simulated exams before testing. More is better.
                               Review your answers and rationales. Also, review your LEM as needed.
                             Your goal is to consistently score in the 80s.
                    Step 4: The online practice exam presents you with questions from the topics covered
                            based on their weighting and emphasis on the actual exam. You will not receive
                            correct answers or rationales for these questions, nor will you receive immediate
                            feedback as you take the exam. You should complete the exam while observing the
                            time limits for the actual exam (2½ hours). Upon completion, you will receive a
                            diagnostic report that identifies topics for further review.

                    Remember, you will not see the answer key and rationale, but the detailed diagnostic
                    breakdown will provide you with clear guidance on areas where further study is required.

                                    How Well Can I Expect to Do?
                                    The exams administered by FINRA are not easy. You must display considerable understanding
                                    and knowledge of the topics presented in this course to pass the exam and qualify for
                                    registration.

                                    Our practice questions were carefully crafted to simulate the actual exam. In addition,
                                    weighting was considered (i.e., the number of questions likely seen on each topic). The
                                    wording might be somewhat different, but if you understand the subject matter, you will be
                                    able to find the correct response when you sit for the test. We often add new questions to
                                    refresh our question bank, sometimes with no direct supporting information pertaining to a
                                    specific question’s subject in our LEM. In that case, there will be a thorough rationale to help
                                    you capture and retain the information you need. Because complex questions require you
                                    to link different concepts together to arrive at a correct answer, dealing with questions not
                                    directly addressed in the LEM will help develop that skill.

                                    If you study diligently, complete all sections of the course, and consistently score at least
                                    85% on the simulated exams, you should be well prepared to pass the exam. However, it is
                                    important for you to realize that merely knowing the answers to our questions will not enable
                                    you to pass unless you understand the essence of the information behind the question.


          TEST-TAKING TIPS
                                    Passing the exam depends not only on how well you learn the subject matter, but also on how
                                    well you take exams. You can develop your test-taking skills—and improve your score—by
                                    learning a few test-taking techniques:
                                     Read the full question
                                     Avoid jumping to conclusions—watch for hedge clauses
                                     Interpret the unfamiliar question
                                     Look for key words and phrases
                                     Identify the intent of the question
                                     Memorize key points
                                     Use a calculator
                                     Avoid changing answers
                                     Pace yourself

                                    Each of these pointers is explained as follows, including examples that show how to use them
                                    to improve your performance on the exam.


                                    Read the Full Question
                                    You cannot expect to answer a question correctly if you do not know what it is asking. If
                                    you see a question that seems familiar and easy, you might anticipate the answer, mark it,
                                    and move on before you finish reading it. This is a serious mistake. Be sure to read the full
                                    question before answering it. Mistakes are often made when assuming too much (or too
                                    little).




S79_LEM_5E.indb 10                                                                                                                  9/1/2020 1:45:49 PM
                                                                                                    Introduction      xi


                     Avoid Jumping to Conclusions—Watch for Hedge Clauses
                     The questions on FINRA exams are often embellished with deceptive distractors as choices. To
                     avoid being misled by seemingly obvious answers, make it a practice to read each question and
                     each answer twice before selecting your choice. Doing so will provide you with a much better
                     chance of doing well on the exam.

                     Watch out for hedge clauses embedded in the question. (Examples of hedge clauses include
                     the terms if, not, all, none, and except.) In the case of if statements, the question can be
                     answered correctly only by taking into account the qualifier. If you ignore the qualifier, you
                     will not answer correctly.

                     Qualifiers are sometimes combined in a question. Some that you will frequently see together
                     are all with except and none with except. In general, when a question starts with all or none and
                     ends with except, you are looking for an answer that is opposite to what the question appears
                     to be asking.


                     Interpret the Unfamiliar Question
                     Do not be surprised if some questions on the exam seem unfamiliar at first. If you have
                     studied your material, you will have the information to answer all the questions correctly. The
                     challenge may be a matter of understanding what the question is asking.

                     Very often, questions present information indirectly. You may have to interpret the meaning
                     of certain elements before you can answer the question. Be aware that the exam will approach
                     a concept from different angles.


                     Look for Key Words and Phrases
                     Look for words that are tip-offs to the situation presented. For example, if you see the word
                     prospectus in the question, you know the question is about a new issue. Sometimes a question
                     will even supply you with the answer if you can recognize the key words it contains. Few
                     questions provide blatant clues, but many do offer key words that can guide you to selecting
                     the correct answer if you pay attention. Be sure to read all instructional phrases carefully. Take
                     time to identify the key words to answer this type of question correctly.


                     Identify the Intent of the Question
                     Many questions on FINRA exams supply so much information that you lose track of what
                     is being asked. This is often the case in story problems. Learn to separate the story from the
                     question.

                     Take the time to identify what the question is asking. Of course, your ability to do so assumes
                     you have studied sufficiently. There is no method for correctly answering questions if you
                     don’t know the material.


                     Memorize Key Points
                     Reasoning and logic will help you answer many questions, but you will have to memorize a
                     good deal of information. Some memorization will be automatic as you go over the material
                     and answer questions; some you will simply have to do systematically.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 11                                                                                                 9/1/2020 1:45:49 PM
          xii        Introduction



                                    Use a Calculator
                                    Any math required to answer a question will be simple math: add, subtract, multiply, and
                                    divide. We recommend using a calculator for math. You may ask for a calculator or ask if you
                                    can use your own. If using your own, only simple math functions are allowed (add, subtract,
                                    multiply, and divide).


                                    Avoid Changing Answers
                                    If you are unsure of an answer, your first hunch is the one most likely to be correct. Do not
                                    change answers on the exam without good reason. In general, change an answer only if you
                                     discover that you did not read the question correctly, or
                                     find new or additional helpful information in another question.


                                    Pace Yourself
                                    Some people will finish the exam early and some do not have time to finish all the questions.
                                    Watch the time carefully (your time remaining will be displayed on your computer screen)
                                    and pace yourself through the exam.

                                    Do not waste time by dwelling on a question if you simply do not know the answer. Make
                                    the best guess you can, mark the question for Record for Review, and return to the question if
                                    time allows. Make sure that you have time to read all the questions so that you can record the
                                    answers you do know.


          THE EXAM

                                    How Do I Enroll in the Exam?
                                    To obtain admission to a FINRA-administered exam, your firm must electronically apply for
                                    and pay a fee to FINRA through its Central Registration Depository (CRD), better known as
                                    the Web CRD®. To take the exam, you must make an appointment with a Prometric Testing
                                    Center as far in advance as possible to get the date you would like to sit for the exam.

                                         www.prometric.com
                                         800.578.6273
                                    You may schedule your appointment 24 hours a day, 7 days a week, on the website. You may
                                    also reschedule or cancel your exam, locate a test center, and get a printed confirmation of
                                    your appointment.

                                    You must have your CRD number available when scheduling your exam. This unique
                                    personal identification number should be provided to you by your employing member firm.
                                    On a cautionary note, failure to show for an examination will be permanently recorded on
                                    your examination history on the Web CRD®.


                                    What Should I Take to the Exam?
                                    Take one form of personal identification with your signature and photograph as issued by
                                    a government agency. No personal items, food, or drink, including coffee and water, are
                                    permitted inside the testing room. Personal items include, but are not limited to, pens, pagers,




S79_LEM_5E.indb 12                                                                                                                  9/1/2020 1:45:49 PM
                                                                                                  Introduction     xiii


                     cellular phones, watches, hats, nonmedical electronic devices, outerwear, purses, and wallets.
                     Personal items must be kept in your assigned locker or returned to your car before the start of
                     your exam. As the testing vendor is not responsible for any personal items, they encourage you
                     to bring only your identification into the Test Center.

                     If you need additional note boards or pens, please alert your proctor. The note boards and
                     pens must be returned at the end of your exam or continuing education session.

                     If you need a calculator for your testing session, please see the Test Center Administrator. You
                     will be provided with a nonprogrammable, nonprinting calculator.


                     Additional Trial Questions
                     During your exam, you may see extra trial questions. These are potential future exam-bank
                     questions being tested during the course of the exam. These questions are not included in
                     your final score.


                     Exam Results and Reports
                     At the end of the exam, your score will be displayed, indicating whether you passed. The next
                     business day after your exam, your results will be transmitted to your firm and to the SRO
                     and state securities commission specified on your application.

           UNIT


             1                           Company Structures




          LEARNING OBJECTIVES
                     When you have completed this unit, you will be able to accomplish the following.
                     ❯ LO 1.a   Recall partnerships and LLCs.
                     ❯ LO 1.b   Relate characteristics of corporations and REITs.
                     ❯ LO 1.c   Identify hedge and equity funds.
                     ❯ LO 1.d   Recall the types of investors and objectives.
                     ❯ LO 1.e   Classify investment strategies and risks.


          INTRODUCTION
                     An investment banker’s job is essentially an advisor to issuers and cer­tain investors in the
                     sale or purchase of companies. To act in this advisory role, it is essential that the investment
                     banker have a thorough understanding of the investment products as well as the needs and
                     investment objectives of his clients. The material covered in Part 1 will lay the groundwork for
                     your success in future parts.


          LESSON 1.1: COMPANY FRAMEWORKS
                     LO 1.a     Recall partnerships and LLCs.

                     Whenever a group of people work together to provide a product or service to customers, they
                     form a unit in the foundation of business: a company. Whether it is a two-person partnership
                     or a corporation of thousands of employees, they are all seeking to do the same thing: generate
                     profits to employ workers and compensate owners.

                     There are many forms under which a company may organize, each with its own advantages
                     and disadvantages. To understand the company’s operations, however, it is important to
                                                                                                                        3



S79_LEM_U1.indd 3                                                                                                   23/05/22 11:33 AM
          4         Unit 1   Company Structures



                                     understand the framework under which it works as well as the risks associated with ownership
                                     in the company.


                                     Partnerships
                                     An unincorporated organization with two or more members is generally classified as a
                                     partnership for federal tax purposes if its members engage in a trade, business, financial
                                     operation, or venture and divide its profits. However, a joint undertaking merely to share
                                     expenses is not a partnership. For example, co-ownership of property maintained and rented
                                     or leased is not a partnership unless the co-owners provide services to the tenants.

                                     An organization is classified as a partnership for federal tax purposes if it has two or more
                                     members and is none of the following:
                                      An organization formed under a federal or state law that refers to it as incorporated or as a
                                       corporation, body corporate, or body politic
                                      An organization formed under a state law that refers to it as a joint-stock company or
                                       joint-stock association
                                      An insurance company
                                      Certain banks
                                      A joint venture, if it is formed as a corporation
                                      An organization wholly owned by a state or local government
                                      An organization specifically required to be taxed as a corporation by the Internal Revenue
                                       Code (e.g., certain publicly traded partnerships)
                                      Certain foreign organizations
                                      A tax-exempt organization
                                      A real estate investment trust
                                      An organization classified as a trust or otherwise subject to special treatment under the
                                       Internal Revenue Code
                                      Any other organization that elects to be classified as a corporation by filing Form 8832

                                                  TAKE NOTE
                                                  A partnership must avoid corporate characteristics. The easiest of the corporate
                                                  characteristics to avoid is continuity of life. Typically, partnerships have a
                                                  predetermined date of dissolution when they are established.


                                     Limited Partnerships (LPs)
                                     Limited partnerships (LPs) are a form of partnership where the operations are run by a general
                                     partner, and capital is contributed by one or more limited partners. These programs offer
                                     investors a share in the income, gains, losses, deductions, and tax credits of the business entity.

                                     Limited partners enjoy several benefits over the general partner:
                                      An investment managed by others
                                      Limited liability
                                      Flow through of income and certain expenses




S79_LEM_U1.indd 4                                                                                                                      23/05/22 11:33 AM
                                                                                      Unit 1   Company Structures        5


                               TAKE NOTE
                               The customer will be considered a limited partner only after the agreement has
                               been signed by the general partner.
                    The greatest disadvantage to limited partners is their lack of liquidity. The secondary market
                    for limited partnership interests is extremely limited; investors who wish to sell their interests        Unit 1
                    frequently cannot locate buyers (i.e., the shareholder’s interest is not freely transferable).


                    Master Limited Partnerships (MLPs)
                    A master limited partnership (MLP) is a limited partnership that is publicly traded.
                    This makes an MLP a more liquid vehicle than other LPs, because MLPs may be traded
                    on exchanges. Like other LPs, MLPs have both general and limited partners, and the tax
                    consequences for any income or loss flow through to the partners.

                    In order for a limited partnership to qualify as an MLP, it must receive at least 90% of
                    revenues from qualified activities (e.g., natural resources or real estate).

                               TAKE NOTE
                               Any publicly traded partnership that does not receive 90% of its revenues from a
                               qualified activity would be taxed the same as a corporation.


                    Limited Liability Companies (LLCs)
                    A limited liability company (LLC) is an entity that combines the aspects of both a partnership
                    and a corporation. LLCs are similar to a partnership in managerial governance (there does not
                    need to be a board of directors), tax treatment (distributions to owners are only taxed once),
                    and life (there is no continual life like a corporation). They are similar to corporations because
                    there is limited liability to owners.


                    Corporations

                    LO 1.b     Relate characteristics of corporations and REITs.

                    A corporation is a legal entity that has been created by the laws of the state (not federal) in
                    which it is incorporated. Upon creation, a corporation becomes a person, with many of the
                    same legal characteristics of a natural person (an individual).

                    Unlike natural persons, however, a corporation is immortal. While companies that are formed
                    as a partnership cease with the death or retirement of a partner, a corporation will continue to
                    exist unless it is dissolved through liquidation.

                    This status as a separate legal entity from its owners (shareholders) provides benefits to its
                    owners. Specifically, it provides the owners with limited liability, where the owner is not held
                    responsible for any of the corporation’s liabilities.

                    Corporations are organized under a board structure, where a board of directors represents
                    the shareholders and oversees and appoints the firm’s management. Taxation of a corporation
                    varies depending on which subchapter of Chapter 1 of the Internal Revenue Code (IRC) it
                    elects to be taxed under.




S79_LEM_U1.indd 5                                                                                                     23/05/22 11:33 AM
          6         Unit 1   Company Structures



                                     C Corporations
                                     A C corporation is a corporation that is subject to the taxation requirements of Subchapter C
                                     of the IRC. This is a very common type of corporation for publicly traded companies because
                                     there are no limitations on the number of shareholders. C corporations, however, have the
                                     disadvantage of double taxation. This occurs because the corporation’s pretax income is taxed
                                     at the corporate tax rate, and all dividends distributed to shareholders are subject to the
                                     shareholders’ personal income tax.


                                     S Corporations
                                     Corporations which are taxed under the provisions in Subchapter S of the IRC are referred
                                     to as S corporations. Unlike C corporations, S corporations do not have double taxation. The
                                     corporation does not pay income tax itself but passes all profits and losses (including its tax
                                     burden) to the shareholders, much like a partnership. To claim this tax benefit, however, the
                                     corporation must meet the following criteria.
                                      The corporation must be domestic.
                                      It must have one class of stock.
                                      There may be no more than 100 shareholders.
                                      Shareholders must be natural persons (entities may not be owners).
                                      Shareholders must be citizens or residents of the United States.
                                      Profits and losses must be distributed proportionally to each shareholder’s percentage of
                                       ownership.

                                                  TAKE NOTE
                                                  Spouses are treated as one shareholder. So, if a husband and wife both have shares
                                                  of an S corporation in their individual accounts, they are considered to be only one
                                                  shareholder.


                                     Real Estate Investment Trusts (REITs)
                                     A real estate investment trust (REIT) is a company that manages a portfolio of real estate
                                     investments to earn profits for shareholders. REITs are normally traded publicly and serve
                                     as a source of long-term financing for real estate projects. A REIT pools capital in a manner
                                     similar to an investment company, and shareholders receive dividends from investment
                                     income or capital gains distributions. As a matter of objectives, REITs can offer excellent
                                     returns in the form of income.

                                     REITs shares some features with limited partnerships but are a different type of business
                                     entity. REITs are traded on exchanges and over the counter and are professionally managed.
                                     Limited partnerships provide pass-through of gains and losses to investors, but REITs do not
                                     provide pass-through of losses.

                                     REITs are organized as trusts where investors buy shares or certificates of beneficial interest
                                     either on stock exchanges or in the over-the-counter (OTC) market. Investors in REITs are
                                     primarily concerned with income. Under the guidelines of Subchapter M of the IRC, an
                                     REIT can avoid being taxed as a corporation by having at least 75% of total investment assets
                                     in real estate, deriving at least 75% of gross income from rents or mortgage interest, and
                                     distributing 90% or more of its net investment income to its shareholders.

                                                                                      Unit 1    Company Structures          7


                              TAKE NOTE
                              Traits of the various company organizations can be summarized in the following table.

                                                C Corp          S Corp      LLC            LP                REIT

                                                                                                                                Unit 1
                               Ownership        Unlimited       Limited
                               Profit/Loss      Stay in Firm    Flows to    Flows to       Flows to          Only profits
                                                                Owners      Owners         Owners            flow to
                                                                                                             owners
                               Tax on           Firm and        Owners      Owners         Owners Only       Owners
                               Distributions    Owners          Only        Only                             Only
                               Owner            Limited         Limited     Limited        Unlimited         Limited
                               Liability                                                   for General

                                                                                           Limited for
                                                                                           Others
                    Whether working with a company to issue its securities or working with an investor to find
                    attractive investments, it is important to understand what types of investors there are, what
                    their objectives are, and what is suitable for them.


                    Hedge Funds

                    LO 1.c    Identify hedge and equity funds.

                    Hedge funds are similar to mutual funds in that investments are pooled and professionally
                    managed, but they differ in that hedge funds are more flexible in the investment strategies
                    employed and are unregulated by U.S. securities laws. They are aggressively managed
                    investment portfolios that use advanced investment strategies generally intended for
                    sophisticated investors. While hedging is the practice of attempting to limit risk, most hedge
                    funds specify generating high returns as their primary investment objective. Some of the more
                    common strategies employed by hedge funds are
                     highly leveraged portfolios,
                     the use of short positions,
                     utilizing derivative products such as options and futures,
                     currency speculation,
                     commodity speculation, and
                     investing in politically unstable international markets.
                    Because hedge funds, unlike mutual funds or investment companies, are unregulated, the
                    very nature of the investment is almost always considered speculative. Most hedge funds are
                    organized as private investment partnerships, allowing them to limit the number of investors
                    or require large initial or minimum investments if they so desire. Some also require that
                    investors maintain the investment for a minimum length of time (e.g., one year) and to that
                    extent they can be considered illiquid.

                                                  the test of a sophisticated investor. They should be considered accredited investors,
                                                  having a minimum annual income and net worth, and have considerable investment
                                                  knowledge.

            LESSON 1.2: INVESTORS
                       LO 1.d Recall the types of investors and objectives.



                       Retail Clients/Individuals
                       Retail customers are an ever-growing portion of the securities marketplace. There are two
                       options for individual investors to gain access to the markets: through individual trading
                       accounts or through managed, pooled accounts such as a mutual fund or a hedge fund. Many
                       of the securities rules and regulations have been created to protect retail customers. Because
                       they can be easily taken advantage of by investment professionals, retail accounts need to
                       be closely monitored, and investments should be carefully selected to fit the individual’s
                       suitability.




S79_LEM_U1.indd 9                                                                                                           23/05/22 11:33 AM
          10         Unit 1   Company Structures



                                      Institutional Investors
                                      Institutional investors are companies that either trade for their own account or manage the
                                      funds of other investors. Institutional investors are covered by fewer protective regulations
                                      than retail customers because it is assumed that they are more knowledgeable and better able
                                      to protect themselves. Institutional accounts are the accounts of
                                       banks,
                                       savings and loan associations,
                                       insurance companies,
                                       registered investment companies, or
                                       registered investment advisers or any other persons (whether a natural person,
                                        corporation, partnership, trust, or otherwise) with total assets of at least $50 million.


                                      Mutual Funds
                                      A mutual fund, or open-end investment company, is a form of pooled investment where
                                      many investors purchase shares in the fund, which then builds a common portfolio. Mutual
                                      funds do not specify the exact number of shares they intend to issue but instead register an
                                      open offering with the SEC. With this registration type, mutual funds can raise an unlimited
                                      amount of investment capital by continuously issuing new shares. Conversely, when investors
                                      liquidate holdings in a mutual fund, the fund’s capital shrinks because the fund redeems the
                                      investor’s shares. The offering never closes because the number of shares the company can offer
                                      is unlimited.

                                      Any person who wants to invest in the company buys shares directly from the company or its
                                      underwriters at the public offering price (POP). A mutual fund’s POP is the net asset value
                                      (NAV) per share plus a sales charge.

                                                   TAKE NOTE
                                                   NAV is computed by deducting the fund’s liabilities from the portfolio’s total assets
                                                   and dividing this amount by the number of shares outstanding.


                                      Qualified Institutional Buyers (QIBs)
                                      A qualified institutional buyer (QIB) is a highly sophisticated investor who may take part
                                      in 144A offerings (discussed later). To qualify as a QIB, the institution must have at least
                                      $100 million under management.


                                      Governments
                                      Governments, like any other organization, seek to maximize returns on unused funds. When
                                      revenues exceed expenses, they may seek to enhance the return on their excess funds by
                                      investing in generally high-quality investments, such as AAA-rated debt securities. Unusual
                                      circumstances may also give governments the incentive to invest in public companies as
                                      well. For example, during the 2008 financial crisis, the U.S. government began holding large
                                      positions in publicly traded companies. While trading in equity securities is not a common
                                      practice for governments, it demonstrates the variety of their potential investments.




S79_LEM_U1.indd 10                                                                                                                         23/05/22 11:33 AM
                                                                                         Unit 1   Company Structures         11


                     Investment Objectives
                     Before working with any investor, it is critical to understand that person’s investment
                     objectives. Without knowing what the investor is attempting to gain from investments, a
                     registered representative cannot make appropriate recommendations for the investor. While
                                                                                                                                  Unit 1
                     investors may have more than one investing objective, investors will usually tend toward
                     one primary strategy. The following details some of the most common investing objectives
                     investors have.


                     Growth
                     Growth investors focus on stocks of companies whose earnings are growing faster than most
                     other stocks and are expected to continue to do so. Because rapid growth in earnings is often
                     priced into the stocks, growth investment managers are likely to buy stocks that are at the
                     high end of their 52-week price range.

                     Aggressive growth investors are a subset of growth investors who specialize in investments
                     that are significantly outperforming their peer group. These investments tend to require a
                     higher risk tolerance. If the company’s earnings continue to significantly outperform their
                     peers, a large return may result. However, if the earnings cease growing at the same pace, the
                     investment may lose a large portion of its value.

                               EXAMPLE
                               KLM Co., a 10-year-old company, is the dominant provider of routers used in
                               telecommunications switching systems. Its earnings have grown in excess of 20%
                               annually for the past seven years. It has $1.2 billion in annual sales, an operating profit
                               of 40% of sales, no debt, and it pays no dividend. The stock trades at 50 times annual
                               earnings.


                     Value
                     Value investors concentrate on undervalued or out-of-favor securities whose prices are low
                     relative to the company’s earnings or book value and whose earnings prospects are believed
                     to be unattractive by investors and securities analysts. Value investment managers seek to
                     buy undervalued securities before the company reports positive earnings. Value investment
                     managers are more likely to buy stocks that are at the bottom of their 52-week price range.

                               EXAMPLE
                               ABC Co., established after World War II, is a metal processor for parts used in the
                               automotive industry. The company has grown by 10% per year for the past 15
                               years but is highly susceptible to downturns in the economy. Twenty percent of its
                               capitalization is in debt used to build a factory, and the stock has paid a quarterly
                               dividend that has increased five times during the past 10 years. Conservatively
                               managed, the company owns assets and cash that exceed the market value of its
                               common stock.


                     Growth at a Reasonable Price (GARP)
                     Growth at a reasonable price (GARP) is a combination of value investing and growth
                     investing. GARP investors look for companies that are showing consistent earnings growth
                     above broad market growth averages while excluding companies that have very high valuation




S79_LEM_U1.indd 11                                                                                                       23/05/22 11:33 AM
          12         Unit 1   Company Structures



                                      (value investing). Generally, GARP investments tend to have low price-to-earnings (P/E) and
                                      P/E to growth (PEG) multiples, showing their preference for undervalued growth companies.


                                      Income
                                      Income investors are mostly interested in receiving regular payments from their investments.
                                      These investors usually have a long time horizon on their investments. Because they are
                                      looking for a steady stream of income, the most common investments for income investors are
                                      bonds and preferred stock. While these investors tend to invest in high-quality securities, they
                                      may attempt to increase payments received by investing in lower-grade securities (e.g., A- or
                                      AA-rated securities instead of AAA-rated securities).


                                      Capital Appreciation
                                      Investors looking for capital appreciation investors do not have a great interest in steady
                                      income from dividends or interest payments. Their primary motive is the long-term growth of
                                      their portfolio. Very frequently, retirement accounts fall in the category of capital appreciation
                                      because they have the goal of steady growth and a long time horizon. The most common
                                      securities found in a capital appreciation portfolio will be common stock with a buy and
                                      hold philosophy. It does not limit the investor from taking advantage of favorable market
                                      conditions for short-term gains.


                                      LO 1.e       Classify investment strategies and risks.



                                      Investment Strategies
                                      Working within the investment objectives of an investor, there may be several strategies that
                                      would be appropriate. Some investors prefer to specialize in one, while others use a wide array
                                      of strategies, depending on the type of investment.


                                      Distressed
                                      Investors seeking distressed securities are attempting to make a gain off the bankruptcy of
                                      a company. Companies in bankruptcy may offer new investors significant returns if the
                                      company returns to good operating condition because their securities are generally offered at
                                      deep discounts (think junk bonds). Purchasers of debt securities of distressed companies are
                                      often provided seniority in the case of liquidation.


                                      Momentum Trading
                                      Momentum trading is based on the belief that when a stock begins to move it will continue
                                      to do so. Momentum traders will look for a stock that has begun to reach its highs and will
                                      buy there, with the expectation that the stock will continue to rise. The exit strategy for a
                                      momentum investment will be to close out the position by selling when the rate of growth
                                      slows down.




S79_LEM_U1.indd 12                                                                                                                     23/05/22 11:33 AM
                                                                                      Unit 1   Company Structures    13


                     Quantitative (Formula Driven)
                     Quantitative trading is a purely mathematical strategy of investing. Using models and
                     formulas, quantitative investors look to identify inefficiencies in the marketplace and utilize
                     algorithms to purchase and sell those securities. The main focus of quantitative trading is price
                                                                                                                             Unit 1
                     and volume within an industry.


                     Arbitrage
                     Arbitrageurs attempt to take advantage of differences in pricing between markets. For
                     example, if there is a difference in the price of a stock between two different exchanges, an
                     arbitrageur would purchase it in the exchange with the lower price and sell it in the exchange
                     with the higher price. Arbitrage generally happen very quickly and is done with a small spread
                     due to intense competition.


                     Short
                     Short sellers are investors who believe a stock will decrease in value. Without owning the
                     stock, the sellers borrow shares and sell them. In the future, the sellers will have to return the
                     borrowed stock by purchasing the shares in the open market and, in the case of having shorted
                     a dividend paying stock, repay all the dividends that were paid by the company during the
                     time the short position was open. If the stock has moved down, the seller makes a profit. If it
                     moved up, however, the seller will face a potentially unlimited loss.


                     Sector Specific
                     Instead of focusing on specific companies, some investors prefer to invest in sectors. They may
                     do this through purchasing several securities in that sector, or they may trade in exchange-
                     traded funds (ETFs) or indexes based on the sector. Common sectors that are traded include
                     technology, health care, and energy.


                     Investment Risks
                     No discussion on investments would be complete without a description of the risks involved.
                     Both investors and investment professionals should be aware of the risks associated with the
                     different types of securities and markets. When dealing with risk, investors should keep in
                     mind that as potential payout from an investment increases, so does the associated risk.


                     Systemic (Market) Risk
                     Systemic, or market, risk is the risk that the entire marketplace will have poor results. This is
                     compared to the risk of any single security losing value. Whereas the risk of a single security
                     can be diversified away, systemic risk cannot. The only way to reduce systemic risk is to
                     completely leave the market.


                     Interest Rate Risk
                     Primarily affecting bonds and preferred stock, interest rate risk is the risk that interest rates
                     will move against your position. For example, if you are the owner of a variable rate bond and
                     market interest rates lower, the interest you receive from your investment will shrink with it.




S79_LEM_U1.indd 13                                                                                                   23/05/22 11:33 AM
          14         Unit 1   Company Structures



                                      Credit Risk
                                      Credit risk is the possibility that an issuer will default on an obligation. Credit risk is generally
                                      measured by credit agencies and is one of the principal determinants of the yield the issuer
                                      must pay. The greater the risk, the higher the required yield needs to be.


                                      Inflation/Deflation Risk
                                      Inflation risk is associated with the value of an asset. If inflation unexpectedly increases, the
                                      value of the asset will decrease, causing a real economic loss in the investment. Deflation
                                      risk is the opposite and mostly affects borrowers. If you are obligated to make payments and
                                      deflation occurs, those payments will be of more worth than they previously were.


                                      Currency (Exchange Rate) Risk
                                      Principally concerning investors in securities denominated outside their native currency,
                                      currency risk is the chance that the exchange rate will change. If, for example, a U.S. investor
                                      purchases bonds denominated in yen, the value of that bond will diminish as the dollar
                                      appreciates against the yen.


                                      Counterparty Risk
                                      Counterparty risk is the risk that the contra side of a trade will not meet its obligations.
                                      For example, if you bought securities, the counterparty risk involved is that the seller does
                                      not make good delivery of the securities you purchased. This risk generally increases as the
                                      transaction becomes less standardized.


                                      Reinvestment Risk
                                      Reinvestment risk is primarily noted in the bond markets but is applicable to any investment.
                                      It is the risk that your returns on any distribution from your investment (e.g., interest
                                      payments) will be lower than that of your initial investment. For example, if you are receiving
                                      5% from a corporate bond, your reinvestment risk is that you will make less than 5% on the
                                      interest payments.

                                                   TAKE NOTE
                                                   One type of debt security is immune to reinvestment risk: zero-coupon bonds.
                                                   Because the bonds do not pay regular interest payments, there is no reinvestment risk
                                                   from changes in interest rates.
                                      One of the most difficult jobs of an investment banker is properly evaluating companies and
                                      their inherent risk. The banker’s valuation will be the basis for the pricing of an initial public
                                      offering (IPO), a bid in a merger or acquisition, or the pricing in a tender offer. It is vital that
                                      investment bankers understand how to obtain and analyze data on the subject company.

                                                                               Unit 1   Company Structures   15

          INTRODUCTION
                     A significant portion of the Series 79 examination will depend on your understanding and
                     ability to recall these financial statements and the financial metrics derived from them. A
                     review of each of these statements and their underlying components will, therefore, comprise
                     most of this unit.


          LESSON 2.1: FINANCIAL STATEMENTS—BALANCE SHEET
                     LO 2.a      Recall balance sheet entries.

                     Regulation S-X requires reporting companies to provide the SEC with copies of their financial
                     statements. These statements must include the balance sheet (or statement of financial
                     condition), income statement, and the statement of cash flows, among others.

                     These statements must also be prepared in accordance with Generally Accepted Accounting
                     Principles (GAAP), a common set of accounting standards established by the Financial
                     Accounting Standards Board (FASB). These statements must be made quarterly on
                                                                                                                     17



S79_LEM_5E.indb 17                                                                                              9/1/2020 1:45:56 PM
          18         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      Form 10-Q, with the year-end statement being audited by an independent, certified
                                      accounting firm on a Form 10-K.

                                      The balance sheet (or statement of financial position) is a snapshot of the financial condition
                                      of a company at a given moment. It shows the three components that make up the company:
                                      assets, liabilities, and stockholders’ equity (net worth). Together, these three components make
                                      up the basic balance sheet equation: assets = liabilities + net worth.

                                                TAKE NOTE
                                                There are two ways to view the basic balance sheet equation:
                                                    assets = liabilities + net worth
                                                    assets – liabilities = net worth


                                      Assets
                                      Assets are the property (both tangible and intangible) of a company that will result in future
                                      economic benefit. On a balance sheet, they are broken into two categories, depending on
                                      liquidity: current assets and long-term (or fixed) assets.


                                      Current Assets
                                      Current assets are assets that can be converted into cash during the next 12 months. Current
                                      assets usually include the following.
                                       Cash and cash equivalents. The most liquid asset on the balance sheet. Cash equivalents
                                        are short-term (under three months) notes with a high credit rating.
                                       Marketable securities. Securities that have a readily available market or mature in less
                                        than one year.
                                       Inventory. The stock of goods to be sold in under one year.
                                       Accounts receivable. Credit sales made to customers where collection is expected within
                                        one year.


                                      Long-Term Assets
                                      Long-term, or fixed, assets are assets that are expected to have a useful life of over one year.
                                      Long-term assets include both tangible and intangible assets. A unique property to fixed assets
                                      is depreciation and amortization, or expensing the asset over its life instead of as an upfront
                                      cost.
                                       Property, plant, and equipment (PP&E). The physical plant, equipment, and land
                                        owned by the company.
                                       Goodwill and intangibles. Goodwill is the value of a company over its net worth.
                                        Generally, goodwill is added to a company’s balance sheet when the company is purchased
                                        for more than its net worth. Intangibles are other assets that are nonphysical, such as
                                        patents and trademarks. Goodwill must be tested at least annually for impairment and be
                                        written down if needed.
                                       Deferred assets. Deferred assets are generally expenses that have been paid before they
                                        will be accounted. For example, if a company pays its rent in advance, a deferred asset
                                        known as prepaid rent expense is created and written down when the rent is due.




S79_LEM_U2.indd 18                                                                                                                     23/05/22 11:39 AM
                                                                                    Unit 2   Company Comparisons      19


                     Liabilities
                     Total liabilities on a balance sheet represent all financial claims by creditors against the
                     company’s assets. Liabilities are placed in two categories: current liabilities (maturing in under
                     12 months) and long-term liabilities (maturing in over 12 months). An accrued liability is an
                     expense that has not been paid, either short or long term.


                     Current Liabilities
                     Current liabilities are corporate debt obligations due for payment within the next 12 months.
                     Current liabilities may include the following.
                      Accounts payable. Amounts owed to suppliers of materials and other business costs.
                      Accrued wages payable. Unpaid wages, salaries, commissions, and interest.
                      Current long-term debt. Any portion of long-term debt due within 12 months.
                      Notes payable. The balance due on equipment purchased on credit or cash borrowed.                    Unit 2
                      Accrued taxes. Unpaid federal, state, and local taxes.
                      Dividends payable journal entry.


                     Long-Term Liabilities
                     Long-term liabilities are financial obligations due for payment after 12 months. Long-term
                     liabilities include the following.
                      Long-term debt. Debts such as mortgages and the portion of bonds due to be paid in
                       over one year.
                      Deferred liabilities. Payments received by a customer prior to the good or service being
                       rendered. These are paid off when the good or service has been provided.


                     Stockholders’ Equity (Net Worth)
                     Stockholders’ equity, or net worth, is the stockholder’s claim on the assets of a company, after
                     the creditor’s claims (liabilities) have been satisfied. Recalling from the balance sheet equation,
                     stockholders’ equity = assets – liabilities. Stockholders’ equity is broken into three distinct
                     parts: capital stock (both preferred and common) at par, capital stock in excess of par, and
                     retained earnings.


                     Preferred Stock
                     Each share of preferred stock has a stated par value from which the dividend is calculated.
                     When each share of preferred stock is sold, the par value of the stock is included in the
                     balance sheet as an entry in the preferred stock portion of ownership equity.


                     Common Stock
                     Like preferred stock, each share of common stock has a par value. Unlike preferred stock,
                     however, this par value has no real effect on the value of the stock and is used solely for
                     accounting purposes. When each share of stock is sold (issued), the par value of that share is
                     added to the common stock category of the balance sheet.




S79_LEM_U2.indd 19                                                                                                     5/18/22 2:25 PM
          20         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      Additional Paid-In Capital
                                      Capital in excess of par, often called additional paid-in capital or capital surplus, is the
                                      amount of money over par value that a company received for selling both common and
                                      preferred stock.

                                                 EXAMPLE
                                                 A company issues 1 million shares of common stock with par value of $0.01 for $15.
                                                 This issuance would result in cash increasing by $15 million, common stock increasing
                                                 by $10,000, and additional paid-in capital increasing by $14,990,000.


                                      Retained Earnings
                                      Retained earnings, sometimes called earned surplus, are profits that have not been paid out in
                                      dividends. Retained earnings represent the total of all earnings held since the corporation was
                                      formed, less dividends paid to stockholders. Operating losses in any year reduce the retained
                                      earnings from prior years.
