          LESSON 2.2: FINANCIAL STATEMENTS—INCOME STATEMENT

                                      Income Statement

                                      LO 2.b    Identify income statement data.

                                      The income statement summarizes a corporation’s revenues and expenses for a fiscal period,
                                      usually monthly, quarterly, year-to-date, or the full year. It compares revenue with costs and




S79_LEM_5E.indb 22                                                                                                                          9/1/2020 1:45:58 PM
                                                                                                      Unit 2   Company Comparisons       23


                                        expenses during the period, culminating in the net income of the firm. This is the financial
                                        statement that generates earnings per share and other profitability metrics.

            Figure 2.2: Income Statement Entries

                     Revenue/Sales
              –      Cost of Goods Sold
              –      Sales, General, & Administrative Expense (SG&A)
              –      Depreciation/Amortization Expense
              =      Operating Income (EBIT)
              –      Interest Expense
              =      Taxable Income (EBT)
              –      Income Tax Expense

                                                                                                                                              Unit 2
              =      Net Income


                                        Revenue (Sales)
                                        The top line of the income statement is sales or revenues. This entry includes all sales (both
                                        cash and credit) resulting from the business’s ongoing operations.


                                        Cost of Goods Sold
                                        Cost of goods sold (COGS) is the direct costs associated with the product or service that
                                        resulted in sales. For a nonservice company, this would be the cost of the inventory sold.

                                        COGS can change significantly due to different methods of valuing inventory. In an
                                        inflationary period, first in, first out (FIFO) inventory valuation will result in lower COGS
                                        and a higher ending inventory value compared to last in, first out (LIFO) or a weighted
                                        average inventory system.


                                        FIFO
                                        When using the FIFO method of inventory accounting, when a sale is made, the item that
                                        was purchased earliest will be expensed. In a price inflationary period, FIFO will cause the
                                        COGS to be lower than if another accounting system is used because the earlier (and thus
                                        least expensive) purchases are expensed. With lower COGS, this results in higher taxes and
                                        net income.


                                        LIFO
                                        When using the LIFO method of inventory accounting, when a sale is made, the item that
                                        was purchased most recently will be expensed. In a price inflationary period, LIFO will cause
                                        the COGS to be higher than if another accounting system is used because the most recent
                                        (and thus most expensive) purchases are expensed. With higher COGS, this results in lower
                                        taxes and net income.


                                        Weighted Average
                                        Weighted average account for inventory is determined by summing the total cost of all
                                        inventory purchased and dividing it by the number of units in inventory. Because this




S79_LEM_U2.indd 23                                                                                                                   9/3/2020 1:11:25 PM
          24         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      averages the lower and higher prices together, it results in smoother accounting for inventory
                                      and will cause COGS to be somewhere between FIFO and LIFO.

                                                 EXAMPLE
                                                 ABC Corp. had no beginning inventory but has made the following inventory
                                                 purchases in January:
                                                     Jan 1         100 units purchased @ $50
                                                     Jan 15        100 units purchased @ $55
                                                     Jan 31        100 units purchased @ $60
                                                 If 150 units were sold during January, the COGS for ABC would be:
                                                     FIFO:         (100 × $50) + (50 × $55) = $7,750
                                                     LIFO:         (100 × $60) + (50 × $55) = $8,750
                                                     Avg:		        (100×$50) + (100×$55) + (100×$60)
                                                                                                     ×150 = $8, 250
                                                                                  300

                                                 TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                 When working through a question that deals with COGS, make sure you know which
                                                 inventory valuation method the firm uses and whether the period has inflationary or
                                                 deflationary prices.

                                                 TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                 A company’s revenue less its COGS is referred to as the gross profit. Gross profit is
                                                 a simple measure of a company’s performance based only on the variable costs of
                                                 production. It does not include any of the indirect costs in production or costs not
                                                 related to production.


                                      Sales, General, and Admin (SG&A) Expenses
                                      Sales, general, and admin (SG&A) expenses are all the expenses that have to do with sales,
                                      general operating, and administrative expenses. These are the expenses that, while not directly
                                      related to the production of a company’s product, are required for the operation of the
                                      company. They include expenses such as
                                       compensation for the sales staff,
                                       advertising,
                                       shipping,
                                       utility bills,
                                       property taxes,
                                       office expenses, and
                                       other miscellaneous overhead expenses.


                                      Earnings Before Interest, Taxes, Depreciation, and Amortization
                                      (EBITDA)
                                      Few investment bankers will look at gross profit as a serious profitability metric. It excludes
                                      too many expenses involved in a company’s operations. The first metric used frequently for




S79_LEM_U2.indd 24                                                                                                                       9/3/2020 1:11:28 PM
                                                                                   Unit 2   Company Comparisons      25


                     analyzing and comparing companies from the income statement is earnings before interest,
                     taxes, depreciation, and amortization (EBITDA). This measure is computed as:

                          EBITDA = revenue – COGS – SG&A

                     EBITDA is an indicator of how well a firm is performing excluding financing costs (interest),
                     noncash expenses (depreciation and amortization), and taxes. EBITDA is a non-GAAP
                     metric; however, it is often used in the valuation of a firm’s operational performance.

                     One advantage of using EBITDA is excluding interest expense when comparing companies
                     both within and across different industries or sectors. Excluding interest expense allows
                     companies that use different types of financing to be more accurately compared. For example,
                     take two companies that are identical in terms of assets, operations, and profitability. One
                     uses debt to finance their operations; the other uses equity. The company that uses debt
                     financing will have larger interest expense, making it appear less profitable than the company
                     using equity. EBITDA removes that element and permits the investment banker to see that
                     operationally, the two companies are identical.                                                        Unit 2
                     The second benefit to using EBITDA as an operating metric is the removal of depreciation
                     and amortization, two noncash expenses that will distort the operational profitability of a
                     company. Companies that have large holdings in fixed assets (whether tangible or not) will
                     have their earnings distorted with their large depreciation and amortization expenses. Like
                     the company that used debt rather than equity in its financing, these companies will look less
                     profitable than their peers simply due to the additional expenses inherent in possessing long-
                     term assets, which is not accurately reflecting the operational effectiveness of the company.

                     A variant of EBITDA is EBITDAR. EBITDAR or earnings before interest, depreciation,
                     amortization, and rent; is computed by adding rent expense (an expense that would be
                     deducted as part of SG&A) back into EBITDA. EBITDAR is best used when comparing
                     companies that have significantly different rent expenses as part of their operating expense,
                     allowing for a fairer comparison.

                               TEST TOPIC ALERT
                               EBITDA and EBITDAR are best used when comparing larger companies that have
                               significant interest, depreciation, amortization, or rent expenses.


                     Amortization/Depreciation/Depletion
                     When a long-lived asset (one with an expected useful life greater than one year) is purchased,
                     the cost is not expensed all at once but is instead carried over the life of the asset. This is a
                     fundamental attribute of the accrual basis of accounting, matching income and expenses in
                     the period earned or incurred rather than when payment is received or made. Following the
                     matching principle, this allows the cost corresponding with the revenue generated by the
                     asset to be expensed at roughly the same time.

                     When a fixed asset is placed on the balance sheet, the asset’s purchase price will be added to
                     fixed assets, and a corresponding entry will be made to liabilities or a shareholder’s equity to
                     keep that basic balance sheet equation balanced. Throughout the life of the asset, however,
                     its value will diminish. For example, a manufacturing plant will lose value throughout its life
                     until it needs to be repaired, replaced, or disposed. Likewise, an ore mine will lose value as
                     the ore is removed. The asset’s loss in value must be recorded on the balance sheet through an
                     entry in a contra-asset account called accumulated depreciation. Entries into this account will
                     decrease the book value of the asset until it has reached the end of its useful life, when the
                     asset will be disposed and written off the financial statements.



S79_LEM_5E.indb 25                                                                                                 9/1/2020 1:46:01 PM
          26         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      When the book value of the asset decreases (a reduction in assets), a corresponding entry
                                      in either liabilities or ownership equity must also be made for the balance sheet to remain
                                      balanced. This balancing entry is done by entering an expense on the income statement,
                                      thereby reducing the company’s equity by the same amount as the reduction in assets. This
                                      expense is depreciation, amortization, or depletion expense.

                                      Amortization, depreciation, and depletion all have the same effect on the assets from
                                      which they are being expensed; however, they differ by the type of asset being expensed.
                                      Amortization is a reduction in the book value of an intangible asset with a finite
                                      lifespan (e.g., a patent). Depreciation is the reduction in book value of a tangible asset
                                      (e.g., machinery). Depletion is the reduction in book value for a natural resource (e.g., a
                                      gold mine). While they are referred to by different names, they each have the same effect,
                                      decreasing the book value of the fixed asset while simultaneously decreasing the retained
                                      earnings of the company.

                                      Amortization, depreciation, and depletion may be expensed in several ways. The simplest form
                                      is straight line. In straight-line depreciation, the asset is depreciated by the exact same amount
                                      each period until the asset reaches the end of its useful life.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                A company purchases a piece of equipment for $100,000 and expects the equipment
                                                to have a useful life of five years. At the end of the five years, the company expects
                                                to dispose of the asset for $10,000. Using straight-line depreciation, the company
                                                will depreciate the asset by (and have a depreciation expense of) $18,000 (100,000
                                                – 10,000 ÷ 5). At the end of the five years, the asset will have been depreciated to its
                                                salvage value, and the asset may be removed from the financial statements upon
                                                disposal.
                                      While common, straight-line depreciation is not the only available method. There are several
                                      forms of accelerated depreciation. The most common forms are double declining and sum-of-
                                      the-years’ digits. These two methods, while different in their execution, will produce similar
                                      effects. Depreciation expense will be higher in earlier periods and lower in later periods. This
                                      allows the company to gain a tax advantage in earlier years (by having larger expenses, taxable
                                      income diminish) and represents that the asset may lose more value in its initial years than it
                                      does in later years.

                                                TAKE NOTE
                                                Depreciation, amortization, and depletion expenses are calculated using estimates of
                                                the useful life and disposal value of the asset and may change throughout the asset’s
                                                life.


                                      Operating Income/Loss (EBIT)
                                      Operating income or EBIT is often spoken of as operating profit. It is a simple gauge
                                      of profitability that omits the corporate capital structure of interest payments and tax
                                      consequences. It is calculated by taking the revenue (sales revenue and nonoperating revenue)
                                      and subtracting the cost of goods sold (COGS), operating expenses, and any nonoperating
                                      expenses. Another way of looking at operating income is earnings before interest and tax
                                      (EBIT). GAAP requires firms to report EBIT in their income statements.




S79_LEM_U2.indd 26                                                                                                                         8/30/23 10:22 PM
                                                                                     Unit 2   Company Comparisons          27


                               TAKE NOTE
                               EBIT and EBITDA both measure a company’s profitability but in different ways. EBIT
                               gauges the regular profitability before the effect of capital structure and taxes. EBITDA
                               gauges the regular cash flow from operations before the effect of capital structure and
                               taxes. Companies that do not have a great deal of capital expenditure would use EBIT
                               for comparison. Companies with large amounts of debt would tend to use EBITDA.


                     Interest Expense
                     Whenever a corporation uses debt to finance its operations, it incurs an interest expense,
                     whether it be directly or indirectly (e.g., with zero-coupon bonds). Interest expense, however,
                     is not considered part of the operating expenses of the corporation, and is thus excluded
                     from operating income. Although interest expense is not an operating expense, it is still
                     tax deductible. Therefore, when interest expense is subtracted from operating income, the
                     resulting figure is the company’s pretax income, or earnings before taxes (EBT).
                                                                                                                                Unit 2

                     Taxes
                     The final item to be expensed before net income is income tax. Income tax expense in its
                     simplest form is computed by multiplying EBT by the firm’s marginal tax rate. In practice,
                     however, tax expense and taxes payable (the amount owed to the taxing authority) may be
                     different due to differences in accounting methods between what is required on an income tax
                     return and what is used in the preparation of financial statements. These differences are called
                     deferred taxes and create a deferred tax asset or a deferred tax liability. It is highly unlikely,
                     however, that you will encounter deferred taxes on the Series 79 exam.

                               TAKE NOTE
                               When referring to taxes in the income statement, reference is only being made to
                               income tax. Other taxes, such as property tax, are included in SG&A.


                     Net Income/Loss
                     The bottom line of the income statement is the firm’s revenues less all its expenses, called the
                     net income (or net loss). This net income (loss) is what is going to be added (subtracted) to
                     retained earnings, and it indicates whether the company generated an accounting profit or loss
                     during the period.


                     Adjustments, Including Extraordinary Items/Nonrecurring Items
                     On a company’s income statement, there may be additional entries “below the line.” These
                     entries are reported after net income (net of taxes) because they do not constitute continued,
                     or ordinary, operations. They include
                      adjustments for previous misstatements,
                      income (loss) from discontinued operations, and
                      extraordinary (unusual and infrequent) items.

                               EXAMPLE
                               If a tornado were to destroy an uninsured factory in Maine, this would be considered
                               an extraordinary item because it is both unusual and infrequent for a tornado to




S79_LEM_U2.indd 27                                                                                                          9/5/23 3:16 PM
          28         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                                strike in Maine. A tornado destroying a factory in Oklahoma, however, may not be
                                                considered extraordinary because tornados in Oklahoma are not unusual.
          Figure 2.3: Income Statement

            LESSON 2.3: FINANCIAL STATEMENTS—CASH FLOW

                       Cash Flow Statement

                       LO 2.c    Classify cash flow statements.

                       The third financial statement required for all publicly traded companies is the statement of
                       cash flows (or cash flow statement). While the balance sheet is a snapshot of the company’s
                       financial condition and the income statement summarizes the transactions that took place
                       over a period, the cash flow statement reconciles the change in the company’s cash position
                       over a period.

                       The cash flow statement differs from the income statement in that it only represents cash
                       transactions. Because of accrual accounting, the income statement potentially shows sales
                       before cash has been exchanged (i.e., sales made on credit). The income statement also shows
                       noncash expenses such as depreciation and amortization, which are only recorded to reduce
                       the value of the underlying assets. The cash flow statement, however, demonstrates how the
                       firm is managing the stream of cash being collected from customers and investors, as well as
                       how the firm is distributing its cash holdings to vendors, creditors, and investors.

                       The cash flow statement is broken into three distinct sections: operating cash flows, investing
                       cash flows, and financing cash flows.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 29                                                                                                      9/1/2020 1:46:02 PM
          30         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



          Figure 2.4: Statement of Cash Flows

                      XYZ, Inc., Statement of Cash Flows
                      For Year Ending December 31, 20X2
           Cash Flow from Operations
                      Net Income                          $200,000
                      Depreciation/Amortization              15,000
                      Increase in Payables                   20,000
                      Decrease in Inventory                  30,000
                      Increase in Receivables               (25,000)
           Cash Flow from Operations                       240,000


           Investing Activities
                      Purchase of Equipment                 (85,000)
                      Sale of Securities                     10,000
           Cash Flow from Investing                         (75,000)


           Financing Activities
                      New Bond Issue                       100,000
                      Cash Dividends Paid                   (10,000)
           Cash Flow from Financing                          90,000


           Beginning Cash                                  250,000
           Total Cash Flow                                 255,000
           Ending Cash                                    $505,000


                                      Operating Cash Flow
                                      Operating cash flow contains all the inflows and outflows of cash related to the company’s
                                      operations. This includes noncredit sales, receivables collected, payments for accounts payable,
                                      and interest expenses.


                                      Operating Cash Inflows
                                      Cash inflows from operations could include the following:
                                       Cash collected from customers through cash sales or collections of receivables
                                       Interest and dividends received from investments
                                       Sales of marketable securities


                                      Operating Cash Outflows
                                      Cash outflows due to operating activities include the following:
                                       Cash paid to vendors through cash purchases or payments of payables
                                       Cash paid to employees




S79_LEM_U2.indd 30                                                                                                                 9/3/2020 1:12:05 PM
                                                                                    Unit 2   Company Comparisons      31


                      Cash purchases of securities used for trading purposes
                      Interest paid to creditors
                      Taxes paid

                               TAKE NOTE
                               While interest expense could be considered a financing cash flow, GAAP requires that
                               it be included in operating cash flow.


                     Calculating Operating Cash Flow
                     There are two methods for calculating operating cash flow: the direct method and the indirect
                     method. While both are allowable under GAAP, if the direct method is used, footnote
                     disclosure of the indirect method must also be included. Most companies, therefore, use
                     the indirect method of computing operating cash flow. While computed in different ways,
                     both the direct and indirect methods will produce the same end result (i.e., CFO is the same            Unit 2
                     regardless of which method is used to calculate it).


                     Direct Method
                     The direct method of calculating CFO is done by summing (subtracting) all the cash inflows
                     (outflows). These cash flows will be presented by categories, such as cash
                      collected from customers,
                      used in production of good,
                      operating expenses,
                      paid for taxes, and
                      paid in interest.


                     Indirect Method
                     The indirect method of calculating operating cash flows can be done in four steps:
                      Start with net income.
                      Subtract gains (add losses) that resulted from financing or investing cash flows (e.g., gains
                        from issuing new debt).
                      Add back all noncash charges to income (e.g., depreciation) and subtract all noncash
                        components of revenue (credit sales).
                      Add or subtract changes to balance sheet operating accounts as follows:
                        –– Increases in the operating asset accounts (uses of cash) are subtracted, while decreases
                           (sources of cash) are added.
                         –– Increases in the operating liability accounts (sources of cash) are added, while
                            decreases (uses of cash) are subtracted.

                               TAKE NOTE
                               The Statement of Cash Flows for XYZ, Inc., in Exhibit C was presented using the indirect
                               method.




S79_LEM_U2.indd 31                                                                                                  9/3/2020 1:12:06 PM
          32         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      Investing Cash Flow (CFI)
                                      Investing cash flow (CFI) is the cash inflows and outflows from the purchase and sale of fixed
                                      assets, generally the firm’s PP&E. Unlike CFO, there is no indirect method for computing
                                      CFI. All cash outflows dealing with investing activities are subtracted from the cash inflows
                                      from investing activities.


                                      Investing Cash Inflows
                                      Cash inflows from investing activities include
                                       proceeds from sale of fixed assets and
                                       principal received from loans.


                                      Investing Cash Outflows
                                      Cash outflows from investing activities include
                                       cash paid to purchase fixed assets and
                                       principal of loans made.


                                      Financing Cash Flow (CFF)
                                      Financing cash flows (CFF) is the cash received from raising capital for the firm and any
                                      outflows resulting from raising the capital. Like CFI, CFF is calculated the same way only
                                      under a direct method by subtracting CFF outflows from CFF inflows.


                                      Financing Cash Inflows
                                      Inflows for financing activities include
                                       principal from issuance of bonds and
                                       cash from issuance of stock.


                                      Financing Cash Outflows
                                      Outflows from financing activities include
                                       dividends paid to preferred and/or common shareholders,
                                       cash paid to repurchase stock, and
                                       principal paid on debt.

                                                TAKE NOTE
                                                Although interest expense is an operating cash flow, dividends paid are not. They are
                                                classified as a financing cash flow.

                     KNOWLEDGE CHECK
                     1.   While the balance sheet is a snapshot of the company’s financial condition and the
                          income statement summarizes the transactions that took place over a period, the
                          cash flow statement does which of the following?
                            A. Reconciles the change in the company’s cash position over a period
                            B. Allows a comparison with companies in different industries
                            C. Computes tax brackets
                            D. Keeps the inventory costs in line
                     2.   How does a cash flow statement differ from an income statement?
                           A. A cash flow statement only represents cash transactions.
                           B. An income statement only comes into play at the end of the accounting
                              period.
                           C. They are both used together as a balance sheet item.
                           D. A cash flow statement can never show a loss.

                                                                                                                      Unit 2
                     3.   The cash flow statement would not contain which of the following?
                           A. Operating cash flows
                           B. Invest­ing cash flows
                           C. Financing cash flows
                           D. Converting cash flows
                     4.   In calculating CFO using the direct method, which of the following is not used?
                            A. Cash collected from customers
                            B. Cash used in production of goods
                            C. Cash paid for taxes
                            D. Subtracting the losses from issuing new debt
                     5.   Outflows from financing activities include all the following except
                           A. dividends paid to preferred shareholders.
                           B. cash paid to repurchase stock.
                           C. principal from issuance of bonds.
                           D. principal paid on debt.

                     Answers
                     1. A The cash flow statement reconciles the change in the company’s cash position over
                          time. LO 2.c

                     2. A The cash flow statement differs from the income statement in that it only
                          represents cash transactions. Because of accrual accounting, the income statement
                          potentially shows sales before cash has been exchanged (i.e., sales made on
                          credit). LO 2.c

                     3. D. There is no entry for converting cash flows in a cash flow statement. LO 2.c

                     4. D Although similar to the indirect method of calculating cash flow, one would
                          subtract gains (add losses) that resulted from financing or investing cash flows
                          (e.g., gains from issuing new debt). LO 2.c

                     5. C Principal received from issuance of bonds is financing cash inflow. LO 2.c
