          LESSON 2.4: ANALYZING THE FINANCIALS
                                      When an investment banker is analyzing a company, raw financial statement numbers are
                                      rarely used. Instead, the investment banker will take the data and calculate different ratios
                                      to compare with other companies in its peer group. By themselves, these ratios have little
                                      meaning, but when used for making comparisons, they are very effective in establishing how a
                                      company fits within its peer group.

                                                TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                This section is incredibly important in your preparations for the Series 79 exam. The
                                                test will assume you know each of these ratios, and will often provide questions with
                                                limited information. You will need to be able to take one (or often more) of these ratios
                                                and use them together to derive an answer.
                                                This tends to be one of the most challenging aspects of the Series 79 exam. Be
                                                prepared to extensively use the exhibit book provided and compare a company to
                                                others, gathering the information from the company’s various financial statements.


                                      Liquidity Metrics

                                      LO 2.d    Define liquidity.

                                      Liquidity is an essential aspect of a company’s operations. The speed at which it can turn assets
                                      into cash allows the company to pay off its obligations and make additional investments.
                                      Greater liquidity allows the company to operate with more flexibility, increases its ability to
                                      grow, and provides investors and creditors with greater confidence in the company’s ability to
                                      meet obligations and pay dividends. Caution, however, must always be taken to maximize the
                                      effectiveness of a company’s cash positions. It is possible for a company to be too liquid by
                                      holding cash positions rather than investing in additional revenue-generating endeavors.

                                      This section will review the basic liquidity measures used by investment bankers to compare
                                      the liquidity of peer companies.


                                      Working Capital
                                      A company’s working capital is defined as its current assets less its current liabilities. Recall
                                      from the discussion on the balance sheet that current assets include any asset that is, or is
                                      expected to become, cash within one year; current liabilities include any liabilities that are
                                      expected to be paid within one year. Working capital is, therefore, a measure of a company’s
                                      short-term solvency. If a company’s working capital is positive, it can expect to adequately
                                      make its short-term payments, whereas if its working capital is negative, the company cannot
                                      pay its current liabilities.

                                      Unlike the other measures presented in this section, working capital is not a ratio. Rather, it
                                      is the dollar amount of current assets over current liabilities. It is difficult for an investment
                                      banker to make a useful comparison of two companies’ liquidities using this measure, because
                                      it will always favor large companies.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The working capital for XYZ, Inc., in 20X2 is $865,000 – $177,100 = $687,900.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 34                                                                                                                          9/1/2020 1:46:03 PM
                                                                                        Unit 2   Company Comparisons      35


                     Current (Working Capital) Ratio
                     Overcoming the bias found in working capital, a better measure for comparing two
                     companies of different sizes is the current ratio. The current ratio, sometimes referred to
                     as the working capital ratio, is derived from information found on the balance sheet and is
                     defined as:

                                              current assets
                          current ratio =
                                            current liabilities

                     This ratio indicates how many dollars in current assets the subject company has for every
                     dollar of current liabilities. It is used in determining short-term liquidity (i.e., the company’s
                     ability to immediately pay its short-term liabilities).

                     A company may be considered solvent if its current ratio is greater than one, indicating that
                     it has more current assets than current liabilities. Current ratios less than one would indicate
                                                                                                                                 Unit 2
                     that its working capital is negative, and it has more current liabilities than current assets.

                     While a high current ratio may seem the most desirable, it is important that, like all other
                     metrics, it never be used by itself but only for comparative purposes between companies in the
                     same peer group. If a company’s current ratio is higher than others in its peer group, it may be
                     a sign that the company is relying too much on long-term debt to fund its operations.

                                TAKE NOTE
                                Recall that current assets are assets that are able to be turned into cash within one
                                year, such as cash, cash equivalents, marketable securities, inventory, and accounts
                                receivable.
                                Current liabilities are debts owed by the company that are payable within one year,
                                such as accounts payable, short-term debt, and the current portion of long-term debt.

                                EXAMPLE
                                The current ratio for XYZ, Inc., in 20X2 is $865,000 ÷ $177,100 = 4.88.



                     Quick (Acid Test) Ratio
                     The quick (or acid test) ratio is a more conservative measure of a company’s short-term
                     liquidity than the current ratio. While the current ratio includes all current assets, the quick
                     ratio most notably omits inventory. It is defined as:
                          quick (acid test) ratio =

                           cash + cash equivalents + accounts receivable + marketable securities
                                                     current liabilities

                     Alternatively, this could be expressed as:

                                                      current assets − inventory
                          quick (acid test) ratio =
                                                          current liabilities

                     The quick ratio is intended to be a more strenuous test on a company’s liquidity. While
                     cash, cash equivalents, and marketable securities are readily able to be converted into cash,
                     inventory is not. Inventory generally will take longer to liquidate, and if it is sold quickly, the




S79_LEM_5E.indb 35                                                                                                      9/1/2020 1:46:35 PM
          36         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      company may not realize its full value from the sale. As a result of omitting inventory, the
                                      quick ratio will always be less than or (if the company has no inventory) equal to the current
                                      ratio, making it a more conservative measure of the company’s liquidity.

                                      A shortcoming of the quick ratio may be its treatment of accounts receivable. The liquidity
                                      of these receivables depends significantly on the credit terms, the credit worthiness of the
                                      customer, and the collection period. While measures such as bad debt expense attempt to
                                      address this, such measures are only estimates and may not fully represent the ability of the
                                      company to quickly collect on its receivables.

                                                 EXAMPLE
                                                 The quick (acid test) ratio for XYZ in 20X2 is ($505,000 + $205,000 + 75,000) ÷ $177,100 =
                                                 4.43. Because the acid test is more conservative than the current ratio, it will always be
                                                 smaller.

                                                 TAKE NOTE
                                                 While the current and quick ratios are used to measure the short-term liquidity of a
                                                 company, they are far from perfect. For example, a company may have a high current
                                                 or quick ratio with most of its current assets being accounts receivable. In this case,
                                                 while the company looks liquid, its collection of the accounts receivable may not be
                                                 completed in time to meet current liabilities, resulting in a liquid-appearing company
                                                 being insolvent in the short term.

                                                 TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                 Both the current ratio and quick ratios are measuring a company’s ability to meet its
                                                 current liabilities. While the ideal value of these ratios will vary between industries,
                                                 they should generally always stay above one. Either a current ratio or quick ratio below
                                                 one would indicate that the company does not have the assets needed to cover its
                                                 current liabilities.


                                      Cash Collection Cycle
                                      A vital part of understanding a company’s liquidity, especially when analyzing a retail
                                      company, is the speed with which it can turn assets into cash. The cash collection cycle
                                      provides the means to determine how quickly a company can generate cash flow. This is
                                      done using three components: days in inventory, days sales outstanding, and days payables
                                      outstanding.
                                      As shown next, the cash collection cycle measures how long it takes to sell inventory, adds
                                      the time it takes to collect on any credit sales, and subtracts the time it takes to pay vendors.
                                      This determines the amount of time it takes for a company to turn its inventory into cash.
                                      Therefore, the cash collection cycle can be represented as:

                                           Cash collection cycle = Days in inventory
                                                                 + Days sales outstanding
                                                                 – Days payables outstanding

                                      To properly understand the cash collection cycle, each component, including any underlying
                                      metrics, must be individually addressed.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 36                                                                                                                            9/1/2020 1:46:35 PM
                                                                                         Unit 2   Company Comparisons        37


                                EXAMPLE
                                JamesCo is a company that both buys and sells its inventory with credit. A review of
                                its financial statements show that its days payables outstanding is 35 days, its days
                                inventory on hand is 20 days, and its days sales outstanding is 30 days. The cash
                                conversion cycle for JamesCo is 20 + 30 – 35 = 15 days.
                                This is interpreted to mean that it takes 15 days from the time JamesCo pays for its
                                inventory to the time it receives payment from its sales. This must be compared with
                                JamesCo’s industry norm.

                                EXAMPLE
                                Combining the time to pay for inventory, time in inventory, and time to collect on
                                credit sales, the cash collection cycle for XYZ, Inc., is: 70.39 + 73.00 – 83.16 = 60.23 days.


                     Receivables Turnover Ratio
                     The receivables turnover ratio is a measure of how efficient a company is at collecting its                    Unit 2
                     accounts receivable and converting it into cash. It is computed using both the income
                     statement and the balance sheet. Its defining formula is:

                                                             net credit sales
                          receivables turnover ratio =
                                                           average receivables

                     This ratio indicates the number of times per year the company collects its receivables. If the
                     receivables turnover ratio is high, it is an indicator that the company is efficient in collecting
                     on its receivables. This can occur when the company
                      has a conservative credit policy;
                      is aggressive in its collections process; or
                      has a high credit-quality customer base (e.g., customers who pay their balances quickly
                        and reliably).

                     While a high receivables turnover ratio would generally be a positive sign for a company, if
                     it exceeds industry norms, it may be a sign that the company is being too conservative in its
                     credit policies. Loosening its policy will decrease its receivables turnover ratio, but at the same
                     time, it would increase sales and revenue, causing a net benefit to the company. On the same
                     note, if the company’s receivables turnover ratio is lower than industry norms, it is not being
                     as efficient as its competitors in converting its receivables into cash.

                                TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                Make sure net credit sales are used when calculating the receivables turnover ratio. If
                                net sales are used, it will include cash sales as well, overstating the ratio.

                                EXAMPLE
                                If XYZ makes 40% of its sales on credit, XYZ’s receivables turnover in 20X2 is
                                          0.4 ×$810, 192
                                                                 = 5.19
                                     ($75, 000 + $50, 000) / 2




S79_LEM_U2.indd 37                                                                                                         9/3/2020 1:15:13 PM
          38         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                                TAKE NOTE
                                                When a ratio compares a balance sheet item to an income statement or cash flows
                                                item, the balance sheet item will generally be an average of the beginning and end of
                                                year values [i.e., (beginning balance + ending balance) ÷ 2].


                                      Days Sales Outstanding
                                      While the receivables turnover ratio is an appropriate method of comparing a company’s
                                      efficiency in collecting its receivables, the cash collection cycle requires knowing how many
                                      days the average receivable is outstanding. To determine the average days receivables are
                                      outstanding, an investment banker would use the days sales outstanding (DSO). It is defined
                                      as:

                                                                              365
                                           days sales outstanding =
                                                                      receivables turnover

                                      Similar to the receivables turnover ratio, you can identify the strengths and weaknesses
                                      of a company’s credit policy by the DSO. If the DSO is low, then the company is being
                                      efficient at collecting on its credit sales. If it is below industry norms, however, it may be
                                      due to excessively strict credit policies. If the DSO high, then the company is not efficiently
                                      converting its receivables into cash, is hurting its cash flow, and is overstating its realized
                                      revenue.

                                                TAKE NOTE
                                                When computing DSO, the 365-day numerator is only used if the receivables turnover
                                                is based on an entire year’s data. If receivables turnover is computed using another
                                                time frame (e.g., a quarter), than you will only use the number of days contained in the
                                                applicable time frame.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The DSO for XYZ in 20X2 is 365 ÷ 5.19 = 70.39. This shows that it takes XYZ about 70
                                                days to collect cash on its credit sales.


                                      Payables Turnover
                                      While it is imperative that a company properly manages its receivables, it is no less important
                                      to a company to properly manage its payables. These payables are short-term lines of credit
                                      extended by vendors to the company. For example, a manufacturing company may purchase
                                      its raw materials from a vendor, but rather than pay cash immediately, a 30-day line of credit
                                      is extended to the manufacturer. The payables turnover ratio measures the speed in which
                                      a company pays its payables. In essence, it is showing how many times per year a company
                                      makes and pays off a credit purchase. The payables turnover ratio is computed as:
                                                                 credit purchases
                                           payables turnover =
                                                                 average payables

                                      Like the receivables turnover ratio, the payables turnover ratio needs to be examined only
                                      when comparing a company to its peers.

                                      A high payables turnover indicates that the company is very fast at paying its creditors. This
                                      can be positive for a company, because it shows that it is credit-worthy, thereby permitting
                                      additional extensions of credit and receive better credit terms. It can be worrisome, however,




S79_LEM_U2.indd 38                                                                                                                         9/3/2020 1:15:31 PM
                                                                                        Unit 2   Company Comparisons     39


                     because the terms of credit may not be used to their full advantage. Because the credit is being
                     paid off with cash quickly, short-term liquidity is suffering (i.e., there is less cash available for
                     use), defeating the purpose of using credit.

                     A low payables turnover ratio would have the opposite implications. This would demonstrate
                     that the company is taking longer to pay off its creditor, providing a disincentive for the
                     vendor to continue extending credit if it is not paid off timely. It does, however, allow the
                     company to maintain cash on its balance sheet, increasing its liquidity and available capital for
                     other opportunities.

                     Like other turnover ratios, it is difficult to determine what is best for an individual company,
                     but it is useful for comparing similar companies and for comparing a company to its industry.

                                EXAMPLE
                                Purchases can be computed as ending inventory + COGS – beginning inventory. If XYZ
                                makes 50% of its purchases on credit, payables turnover for XYZ is:                             Unit 2
                                     0.5 ×($70, 000 + $425, 000 − $100, 000)
                                                                               = 4.39
                                            ($55, 000 + $35, 000) / 2

                                TAKE NOTE
                                Unlike inventory turnover and receivables turnover, it is generally better to have a
                                longer payables turnover (when looking strictly at liquidity), while remaining near
                                industry norms.


                     Days Payables Outstanding
                     The number of days between a company’s purchase of a good and its payment for that good
                     is the days payables outstanding. This is calculated by multiplying the inverse of the payables
                     turnover by 365, otherwise written as:
                                                                 365
                           days payables outstanding =
                                                           payables turnover

                     Having a longer days payables outstanding may be a positive sign for a company due to
                     the increase in liquidity from holding onto its cash longer; however, it may also indicate an
                     inability to pay its bills, exposing its lack of liquidity and frustrating its creditors.

                                EXAMPLE
                                With a payables turnover of 4.39, XYZ’s days payables outstanding is 83.16 days.


                     Inventory Turnover
                     Along with receivables, companies need to manage how quickly they can convert their
                     inventory into cash. The inventory turnover ratio is a tool that an investment banker can use
                     to determine the company’s efficiency in this regard. The inventory turnover ratio is defined
                     as:

                                                   cost of goods sold
                           inventory turnover =
                                                   average inventory




S79_LEM_U2.indd 39                                                                                                     9/3/2020 1:15:51 PM
          40         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      The inventory turnover ratio calculates the number of times in a year a company will sell and
                                      replace its inventory. When the inventory turnover is high, a firm is moving items in and out
                                      of its inventory quickly. Likewise, the lower the inventory turnover ratio, the fewer times the
                                      company is selling and replacing its inventory.

                                      Inventory turnover ratios will vary widely between industries. For example, a grocery store will
                                      have a very high inventory turnover due to its small margins and its need to sell significant
                                      volumes to achieve profitability. A high-end automotive manufacturer, however, would have
                                      a much lower inventory turnover since it is a durable good with greater margins for each
                                      product sold.


                                      Days in Inventory
                                      As seen in the receivables turnover ratio and days sales outstanding, the inventory turnover
                                      ratio can be converted from an efficiency metric to the average number of days products are
                                      held in inventory—days in inventory. Days in inventory is calculated as:

                                                                         365
                                           days in inventory =
                                                                  inventory turnover

                                      Just as when there is a high inventory turnover ratio, a low days in inventory would show
                                      that products are being sold and replenished quickly. While this may be very appropriate
                                      and expected from a company selling consumable goods (e.g., a grocery store), it may be an
                                      indicator that a durable goods company has insufficient supply.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                XYZ Inc.’s inventory turnover ratio indicated that it replenishes its inventory five times a
                                                year. The days in inventory is, therefore, calculated as 365 ÷ 5 = 73 days.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The inventory turnover for XYZ Inc. in 20X2 is
                                                              $425, 000
                                                                                 = 5.00
                                                     ($ 70, 000 + $100, 000) / 2


                                                TAKE NOTE
                                                It is important to remember what is in the numerator of turnover ratios. A figure from
                                                the income statement will be in the numerator, while an average balance sheet figure
                                                will be in the denominator. COGS is in inventory turnover because it is the best figure
                                                in the income statement relating to inventory levels.


                                      Debt-to-Equity
                                      A company’s financial leverage is often measured using its debt-to-equity ratio. This ratio
                                      illustrates the relationship between how much debt is used to fund the company’s assets
                                      compared to equity. There are two common ways of measuring debt-to-equity: total debt-to-
                                      equity and long-term debt-to-equity.




S79_LEM_U2.indd 40                                                                                                                         9/3/2020 1:16:03 PM
                                                                                       Unit 2   Company Comparisons         41


                     Total
                     Total debt-to-equity measures a company’s entire debt position relative to its shareholder’s
                     equity. It is most easily conceptualized as how many dollars of debt there are for every dollar
                     in equity.
                                                    short-term debt + long-term debt
                          total debt-to-equity =
                                                           shareholder’s equity

                     As with all other metrics, it is important to view it in relation to other companies and not
                     simply on its own. While it may be normal for a utility company to use more debt than equity
                     to fund its activities (D/E ratio > 1), such a high ratio may be a sign of financial instability for
                     a retailer who normally has low D/E ratios.

                                TAKE NOTE
                                Total liabilities is frequently used as a substitute for the numerator in computing the            Unit 2
                                total debt-to-equity ratio.

                                EXAMPLE
                                The total debt-to-equity for XYZ, Inc., is ($115,000 + $135,000) ÷ $3,434,900 = 0.07.


                     Long-Term
                     As opposed to total debt-to-equity, long-term debt-to-equity only takes into account the
                     long-term debt (debt maturing in over one year) of the company’s balance sheet.
                                                            long-term debt
                           long-term debt-to-equity =
                                                          shareholder’s equity

                     Long-term debt-to-equity emphasizes the leverage a company has in liabilities that will come
                     due in over a year. Rather than viewing a company’s short-term liquidity, long-term debt-to-
                     equity is used to view the company’s long-term solvency and status as an ongoing concern. If
                     this is lower than the industry norm, it may be an indicator that the company is relying too
                     much on short-term debt.

                                EXAMPLE
                                The long-term debt-to-equity ratio for XYZ, Inc., is $135,000 ÷ $3,434,900 = 0.04.


                     Debt-to-Capital
                     Similar to debt-to-equity, debt-to-capital measures the percentage of debt to all capital raised.
                     This is frequently measured as both total debt-to-capital and long-term debt-to-capital.


                     Total
                     Total debt-to-capitalization measures a company’s financial leverage by determining the
                     percentage of the firm’s total capital tied to debt obligations. It is calculated as:
                                                    short-term debt + long-term debt
                          total debt-to-capital =
                                                     total debt + stockholder’s equity




S79_LEM_U2.indd 41                                                                                                        9/3/2020 1:16:32 PM
          42         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The total debt-to-capital of XYZ, Inc., is ($115,000 + $135,000) ÷ ($115,000 + $135,000 +
                                                $3,434,900) = 0.07.


                                      Long-Term
                                      Long-term debt-to-capitalization measures the amount of a company’s capital tied in long-
                                      term debt. This is primarily used to identify whether the company is using too much or too
                                      little long-term debt in its capital structure.

                                                                                     long-term debt
                                           long-term debt-to-capital =
                                                                          long-term debt + stockholder’s equity


                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The long-term debt-to-capital of XYZ, Inc., is $135,000 ÷ ($135,000 + $3,434,900) = 0.04.
                                                This calculation only takes into account long-term capital (long-term debt and equity).

                                      While debt-to-capital and debt-to-equity ratios are similar, they represent different aspects of a
                                      company’s liquidity and solvency. Debt-to-equity demonstrates how many dollars of debt the
                                      company has for each dollar of equity. Debt-to-capital, however, illustrates the percentage of
                                      the company’s capital structure that is debt.


                                      Net Debt
                                      A way of looking at how much debt a company has is by calculating its net debt, defined as:
                                           net debt = short-term debt + long-term debt − cash and cash equivalents

                                      The net debt shows how much debt the company would continue to have if it used all its cash
                                      and cash equivalents to cover its debt. This measure allows an analysis of how well covered the
                                      company’s debt is compared to its current cash holdings. The higher a company’s net debt, the
                                      more leveraged its financial position. If, however, the company has a cash position that largely
                                      offsets its debt, the company is in a stronger position. For example, if interest rates fall, the
                                      cash position would allow the company to retire debt early (through either a call provision or
                                      a tender offer).

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The net debt of XYZ, Inc., for 20X2 is ($115,000 + $135,000) – $505,000 = –$255,000. This
                                                indicates that if XYZ desired (and was able to in the debt covenants), it could pay off all
                                                its debt with the cash on hand.


                                      Profitability Metrics

                                      LO 2.e    Identify profitability measures.

                                      As stated earlier, the goal of any company is to make profits. While a company may be
                                      profitable, it is useful to determine how profitable the company is, and how the company
                                      became profitable. Profitability can be measured in many ways. It may be expressed as a return
                                      on the company’s assets or a return on invested capital. Profits may arise from many sources,




S79_LEM_U2.indd 42                                                                                                                            9/3/2020 1:16:49 PM
                                                                                  Unit 2   Company Comparisons         43


                     such as efficiently using the firm’s assets or by minimizing certain expenses. Whatever the
                     method used, profitability remains a key component in any investment banker’s analysis of a
                     company.


                     Earnings Per Share (EPS)
                     Earnings per share (EPS) is possibly the most cited measure of a company’s profitability.
                     It measures how much of a period’s earnings are available to a common shareholder. It is
                     typically reported by every listed company on an exchange.

                     There are two types of EPS: basic EPS and diluted EPS. Basic EPS is calculated as:
                                      net income− preferred stock dividends
                          EPS =
                                  average outstanding number of common shares


                                                                                                                            Unit 2
                     Note that the numerator subtracts preferred dividends from net income. This is due to the
                     requirement of all preferred shareholders to receive their entire stated dividend before the
                     payment of a dividend to common shareholders.
                     Diluted EPS is the EPS if all convertible securities are converted into common stock.
                     Convertible securities would include convertible preferred stock, convertible bonds, rights,
                     warrants, and any outstanding employee stock options. With the addition to the number of
                     shares outstanding, diluted EPS will always be less than basic EPS, except in the rare case of
                     a company having no convertible securities. Both basic and diluted EPS are required to be
                     included in the income statement under GAAP.

                               EXAMPLE
                               EPS are often found on the income statement. However, it can be calculated manually.
                               XYZ’s LTM EPS is $200,000 ÷ 600,000 = $0.33.
                               Note that XYZ does not have any preferred dividends being paid; otherwise, they
                               would have been removed from net income before calculating EPS.

                               EXAMPLE
                               A company has 1,000 common shares of stock outstanding. It has also issued $5,000
                               worth of convertible debentures with a conversion price of $50. The diluted number of
                               shares for this company is:

                                                5,000
                                    ​1,000 + _
                                             ​       ​ = 1,100​
                                                 50

                     Earnings Yield
                     Earnings yield measures the amount of return each dollar of investment receives. It is defined
                     as:

                                                    EPS
                                             _______________
                          ​earning yield = ​                     ​​
                                             current share price

                     The most common use of earnings yield is to compare a company’s or a market’s returns to the
                     debt markets. Earnings yield is expressed as a percentage. For example, if a company’s earnings
                     yield is lower than the yield found in debt markets, it is an indicator that the company is




S79_LEM_U2.indd 43                                                                                                     5/18/22 2:39 PM
          44         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      overvalued (i.e., an investor would be paying too much for the stock). This results from
                                      prices having an inverse relationship with yields (as discussed in Unit 1). Equities with lower
                                      earnings yields than debt yields would be especially worrisome for the investor because debt
                                      securities are expected to naturally have lower yields, thanks to their lower risk.

                                                 TAKE NOTE
                                                 Earnings yield is the reciprocal of the P/E ratio.

                                                 EXAMPLE
                                                 With a current market price of $9.53, the earnings yield for XYZ’s LTM EPS is $0.33 ÷
                                                 $9.53 = 3.50%.


                                      Free Cash Flow Yield
                                      Free cash flow (FCF) yield, like earnings yield, is a measure of the return for each dollar of
                                      investment. The difference between the two is that while earnings yield measures return using
                                      EPS, FCF yield measures return by FCFs per share. It is calculated as:
                                                                        free cash flow per share
                                            free cash flow yield =
                                                                     current market price per share

                                      When calculating FCF yield, it is vital to understand what FCF per share is. FCF is operating
                                      cash flows (CFO) less any capital expenditures. Alternatively, if the cash flow statement is not
                                      available, FCF can be computed as:

                                           FCF = EBIT × (1 – tax bracket) + depreciation and amortization – change in working
                                                 capital – capital expenditures

                                      Once FCF has been determined, it is then divided by the number of shares to make a per
                                      share comparison in FCF yield.

                                      Investors will generally prefer a higher FCF yield because it would indicate more FCF is
                                      available for every dollar spent on the investment. Like earnings yield, this can be used to
                                      compare an equity investment to interest rates.

                                                 EXAMPLE
                                                 The FCF yield for XYZ is [($240,000 – $85,000) ÷ 600,000] ÷ $9.53 = 2.71%.
                                                 Note that the FCF is operating cash flows less capital expenditures.


                                      Asset Turnover Ratio
                                      While the previous ratios have been measuring the rate of return on an investment, the asset
                                      turnover ratio is a measure of how efficiently assets are being used to generate revenue. It is
                                      defined as:

                                                                   sales
                                            asset turnover =
                                                               average assets

                                      The asset turnover ratio shows how much revenue each dollar in assets produces. Companies
                                      that have a high asset turnover ratio are efficiently using their assets, and each dollar in assets
                                      is generating a large amount of revenue. Conversely, if the asset turnover ratio is low, it




S79_LEM_U2.indd 44                                                                                                                       9/3/2020 1:17:30 PM
                                                                                      Unit 2   Company Comparisons         45


                     indicates the company is not effectively using its assets, which may indicate problems in the
                     company’s processes or management.

                                EXAMPLE
                                Asset turnover for XYZ in 20X2 is:
                                                 $810,192
                                                                       = 0.22
                                     ($3, 905, 000 + $3, 587, 000) / 2


                     Equity Turnover Ratio
                     Like the asset turnover ratio, the equity turnover ratio is a measure of a company’s efficiency.
                     In this case, however, rather than measuring the efficiency of using assets to generate sales, it is
                     a measure of the efficiency of using equity to generate sales. It is defined as:

                          equity turnover =
                                                     sales                                                                        Unit 2
                                                average equity

                     If a company has a high equity turnover ratio, it demonstrates that the company’s
                     management is efficiently using its equity to generate sales. This equity could be capital
                     infusions through either the issuance of equity securities or retained earnings.

                                EXAMPLE
                                Equity turnover for XYZ in 20X2 is:

                                                $810, 192
                                                                         = 0.24
                                     ($3, 434, 900 + $3, 369, 000) / 2

                                TAKE NOTE
                                Whenever the work turnover is used in a ratio, it will be net sales divided by the
                                average of what is being turned over (e.g., asset turnover is sales divided by average
                                assets).


                     Profit Margins
                     The various profit margins are all based off income statement information. They indicate the
                     percentage each line item is of sales. Therefore, the line item will always be the numerator, and
                     sales will always be the denominator.


                     Gross Margin
                     Recall that gross profit is sales less the COGS. The gross margin is, therefore, the percentage
                     gross profit in net sales, and is calculated as:

                                             gross profit
                          gross margin =
                                                sales

                     Gross margin identifies the percentage of sales that remains after removing the COGS to pay
                     the company’s remaining expenses. The higher the gross margin is, the more funds will be
                     available for other expenses and the shareholders.




S79_LEM_U2.indd 45                                                                                                       9/3/2020 1:17:49 PM
          46         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      Gross margin, like many of the margins to follow, will vary greatly depending on industry.
                                      Industries with high COGS (e.g., retailers) will have lower gross margins than those with low
                                      COGS (e.g., service companies).

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                XYZ’s 20X2 gross margin is $385,192 ÷ $810,192 = 47.54%.


                                      Operating Margin
                                      Operating margin, or operating profit margin, is the percentage of sales remaining after
                                      removing operating expenses. Operating margin is defined as:

                                                                 operating income
                                           operating margin =
                                                                       sales

                                      Operating margin, expressed as a percentage, identifies the amount of sales left after deducting
                                      all operating costs. The remaining balance of sales will be available for use in paying the
                                      company’s interest expense and taxes.
                                      This would be an important metric for a potential creditor to view, because it illustrates the
                                      company’s profitability before paying creditors. A higher operating margin would be cause for
                                      a higher credit rating due to the diminished financial, or credit, risk.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                XYZ’s operating margin is ($385,192 – $65,000) ÷ $810,192 = 39.52%.
                                                Note that operating income is EBITDA less any nonoperating income.

                                                TAKE NOTE
                                                Remember the formula for operating income:
                                                      sales – COGS – SG&A – depreciation and amortization


                                      Pretax Margin
                                      Like all other margins, pretax margin illustrates the percentage of sales remaining after all
                                      nonextraordinary expenses are deducted. It is defined as:

                                                      earnings before taxes (EBT)
                                           pretax =
                                                                 sales

                                      A key advantage to pretax margin is that it illustrates the profitability of a company before its
                                      application of income tax. Because tax rates are based off the company’s EBT, pretax margin
                                      shows how well a company is managing its controllable expenses.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                Pretax margin for XYZ in 20X2 is $307,692 ÷ $810,192 = 37.98%.




S79_LEM_U2.indd 46                                                                                                                    9/3/2020 1:18:08 PM
                                                                                   Unit 2   Company Comparisons     47


                     Net Margin
                     The final margin that can be calculated from the income statement is the net margin, or net
                     profit margin. Net margin is defined as:

                                         net income
                          net margin =
                                            sales

                     Net margin is the percentage of sales remaining after all nonextraordinary expenses have
                     been deducted and shows the overall profitability of the company. A larger net margin would
                     indicate that additional returns are available to the shareholders through either dividends
                     or the company retaining the earnings. Additionally, if net margin is high, the company is
                     demonstrating that it has good expense management and that it is able to convert sales into
                     profits with minimum expenses.


                                                                                                                           Unit 2
                               EXAMPLE
                               XYZ’s net margin in 20X2 is calculated as $200,000 ÷ $810,192 = 24.69%.


                     Return on Investment (ROI)
                     A common requirement for investors is that they receive a reasonable return on their
                     investment. This return is defined as:
                                                    gains from investment − cost of investment
                          return on investment =
                                                                cost of investment

                     Return on investment (ROI) is almost always expressed as a percentage. The higher the return,
                     the more favorable the investment.

                     This is not, however, the only way returns are measured. While ROI shows how profitable an
                     investment is, many other returns can be used when analyzing a company’s ability to generate
                     income.

                     When viewing a company, the return will generally be the company’s net income and will
                     serve as the numerator in the fraction. The denominator will be the average of a balance sheet
                     account. These metrics will demonstrate the net income generated by the metric’s eponymous
                     resources.


                     Return on Assets (ROA)
                     Return on assets (ROA) is a measure of how efficiently a company can use its assets to
                     generate profits. ROA is defined as:

                                                net income
                          return on assets =
                                               average assets

                     Companies that are generating a high ROA are effectively using their assets. In other words,
                     each dollar of a high ROA company’s assets is able to generate more net income relative to
                     their peers. Conversely, companies with low ROA are not using their assets effectively. This
                     may indicate that either the assets are unable to be used well (e.g., an out-of-date production
                     facility) or that the management is not properly controlling assets to maximize production
                     and profitability.




S79_LEM_U2.indd 47                                                                                                9/3/2020 1:18:35 PM
          48         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The ROA for XYZ in 20X2 is:

                                                                   $200,000
                                                     ​​ ________________________
                                                             ​ = 5.34%​
                                                          
                                                        ​($3,905,000 + $3,587,000 )​/ 2

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                ABC Co. has $100 million in assets, and its net income is $30 million. XYZ, Inc., a
                                                company in the same sector, has $50 million in assets and a net income of $20 million.
                                                The ROA for ABC is 30 ÷ 100 = 30%, and XYZ is 20 ÷ 50 = 40%. While ABC may be the
                                                larger company both in terms of assets and profits, XYZ is more efficient in turning
                                                those assets into profits.


                                      Return on Equity (ROE)
                                      A company’s return on equity (ROE) is a measure of how efficiently a company turns its
                                      equity capital contributions and retained earnings into profits. It is calculated as:
                                                                          net income
                                           ​return on equity = ​ ______________________
                                                                    
                                                                                              ​​
                                                                 average shareholder’s equity
                                      Shareholders demand that managers of the company maximize the use of their money, and,
                                      therefore, they require as high an ROE as possible. If a company has a lower ROE than its
                                      peers, its management has underperformed in its duty of effectively using the shareholders’
                                      contributions relative to its peers, which makes the company less attractive to future
                                      investment.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The ROE for XYZ in 20X2 is

                                                                $200,000
                                                      ________________________
                                                         ​ = 5.88%​
                                                      ​​   
                                                     ​($3,434,900 + $3,369,000 )​/ 2


                                      Return on Invested Capital (ROIC)
                                      Return on invested capital (ROIC) is the return on all the capital invested (both debt and
                                      equity) in a company. Because ROIC includes debt, the return is not based on net income,
                                      but rather a tax-adjusted EBIT to account for the returns to debt holders through interest
                                      payments. Like the other return metrics, ROIC is a measure of how efficiently a company is
                                      using its invested capital (both equity and debt) in generating returns, or income. ROIC is
                                      generally defined as:
                                                       EBIT × (1 − tax rate)
                                           ​ROIC = ​ _________________________
                                                                            ​​
                                                           invested capital
                                                EXAMPLE

                                                     ___________________________________
                                                     ​​                                          ​ = 5.65%​
                                                             $115,000 + $135,000 + $3,434,900

                                TAKE NOTE
                                Recall that invested capital is total debt + equity.


                     Total Expense Ratio
                     A profitability metric that is unique to fund is the total expense ratio (TER). This may be
                     applied to investment products such as mutual or hedge funds. The TER is a measure of how
                     expensive a fund is relative to its size in assets. TER is specifically defined as:

                                                  total expenses
                          total expense ratio =
                                                  average assets

                     Unlike the other measures addressed in this section, a lower TER would be advantageous to
                     investors. It would demonstrate that a fund was able to keep its expenses low for each dollar of
                     assets in the fund, demonstrating efficiency in fund management.
                                                                                                                                Unit 2
                                TAKE NOTE
                                Fund expenses may come in many forms. There may be purchase fees, maintenance
                                fees, management fees, redemption fees, and custodian fees. These fees should be
                                disclosed to investors, allowing investors to compare the expenses of funds regardless
                                of fund size.


                     Leverage Metrics

                     LO 2.f     Interpret leverage.

                     Leverage is the degree to which a company is operating from borrowed money. While high
                     leverage can increase the potential returns to shareholders, it also increases the probability that
                     the company will not be able to make interest payments and default on its obligations. The
                     following metrics are used in determining the extent to which a company is leveraged.


                     Interest Coverage Ratio
                     The interest coverage ratio measures a company’s ability to make its interest payments. It is
                     defined as:

                                                           EBIT
                          interest coverage ratio =
                                                      interest expense

                     The interest coverage ratio identifies how much money is available for each dollar of interest
                     expense. The higher the ratio, the more money is available to pay creditors the interest due.
                     The lower the ratio, the more leveraged the company. When a company finds itself over-
                     leveraged, it becomes exposed to heightened credit risk and may find it difficult, or more
                     expensive, to secure future financing.

                                EXAMPLE
                                XYZ’s interest coverage ratio for 20X2 is ($385,193 – $50,000 – $15,000) ÷ $12,500
                                = 2,562%. This value is high but can be found in firms that rely very little on debt
                                financing.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 49                                                                                                     9/1/2020 1:51:10 PM
          50         Unit 2   Company Comparisons



                                      Debt to EBITDA
                                      Debt to EBITDA is a measure of a company’s ability to pay off its debt. It is defined as:

                                                                   debt
                                           debt to EBITDA =
                                                                 EBITDA

                                      A way to conceptualize debt to EBITDA is that it represents the amount of time it would take
                                      to pay off all the company’s debt. EBITDA is used because it does not include the noncash
                                      expenses of depreciation and amortization. EBITDA is frequently used to substitute available
                                      cash for that reason. If a company has a high debt to EBITDA ratio, the company will take
                                      longer to pay off its debts, is more leveraged, and bears heightened credit risk. This may result
                                      in a downgrade of the company’s credit rating and an increased required rate of return for any
                                      securities issued by the company. On the other hand, if debt to EBITDA is very low, relative
                                      to the company’s peers, it may not be using debt financing to its full extent and may be
                                      missing growth opportunities.

                                                EXAMPLE
                                                The debt-to-EBITDA ratio for XYZ in 20X2 is ($115,000 + $135,000) ÷ ($385,192 – $50,000)
                                                = 0.75.


                                      Net Debt to EBITDA
                                      Net debt to EBITDA is very similar to debt to EBITDA. The difference is that cash and cash
                                      equivalents are subtracted from the debt in the numerator, resulting in net debt.

                                                                      debt − cash and cash equivalents
                                           net debt to EBITDA =
                                                                                 EBITDA

                                      Like debt to EBITDA, net debt to EBITDA represents the amount of time it would take to
                                      pay off all the company’s debt if, in addition to using EBITDA, all-cash and cash equivalents
                                      on hand were used to pay off the debt as well.
