          INTRODUCTION
                     The essence of what an investment banker does is evaluating companies. Whether assisting a
                     client in pricing a new issue of securities or determining the bid in an acquisition, investment
                     bankers are valuing how much a company and its securities are worth. Valuating a company
                     will require calculating various valuation metrics. Like with any other metric calculated from
                     the financial statements, these ratios mean little by themselves but can be extremely useful in
                     conjunction with each other and as a comparison to similar companies.




                                                                                                                        53



S79_LEM_5E.indb 53                                                                                                 9/1/2020 1:51:32 PM
          54         Unit 3   Company Valuations



          LESSON 3.1: STANDARD VALUATION TOOLS

                                      Enterprise Value (EV)

                                      LO 3.a       Define enterprise value (EV).

                                      Enterprise value (EV) is the theoretical takeover price of a company. It is calculated as:

                                             EV = Market capitalization
                                                + Total debt
                                                + Capital leases
                                                + Minority interest
                                                – Cash and cash equivalents

                                                   TAKE NOTE
                                                   The market capitalization (market cap) of a company is the current market value of its
                                                   stock multiplied by the number of shares. For example, if a company had 1,000 shares
                                                   outstanding with a market price of $25, its market cap is $25,000.
                                      EV is the combination of all the different parts of a company for which a purchaser would
                                      need to pay. The calculation starts with the purchase of all the company’s equity, valued at its
                                      market capitalization of both common and preferred stock. Added to that is all of the firm
                                      debt, both long- and short-term, which the purchaser would be assuming; capital leases for
                                      which the purchaser will now also be liable; and any minority (noncontrolling) interest in
                                      subsidiary companies. Cash and cash equivalents are subtracted because the purchaser would
                                      gain access to all the cash and cash equivalents upon purchase.

                                                   EXAMPLE
                                                   XYZ’s EV on December 31, 20X2, is calculated as (600,000 × $9.53) + $115,000 + $135,000
                                                   + $360,915 – $505,000 = $5,823,915.


                                      EV to EBITDA
                                      EV to EBITDA is a very popular multiple of a company’s value, much like P/E. It looks at
                                      the cost of a company (in this case, the EV) to its earnings (in the form of EBITDA). EV to
                                      EBITDA is defined as:

                                                                         enterprise value
                                            EV/EBITDA multiple =
                                                                            EBITDA

                                      EV/EBITDA has the advantage of not having capital structure or taxation as part of the
                                      valuation, facilitating comparison between a greater number of companies, including the
                                      capital intensive and the highly leveraged. When comparing two companies using EV/
                                      EBITDA, adjustments also do not need to be made for differing tax policies or depreciation
                                      and amortization schedules. EV/EBITDA may also be appropriate as a valuation tool for




S79_LEM_5E.indb 54                                                                                                                          9/1/2020 1:51:43 PM
                                                                                            Unit 3   Company Valuations     55


                     companies with low profit margins. While a company may have a net loss (making P/E
                     meaningless), a company generally has a positive EBITDA that permits comparison.

                                                       enterprise value
                          EV/EBITDA multiple =
                                                          EBITDA

                     When EV/EBITDA is low, it is a sign that the company may be undervalued.

                               EXAMPLE
                               The EV/EBITDA for XYZ at the end of 20X1 is $5,823,915 ÷ ($385,192 – $50,000) = 17.37.


                     EV to Sales
                     EV/sales is a measure of how expensive a company is for every dollar of sales. It is used as a
                     more generalized version of EV/EBITDA. When a company’s EV/sales is low compared to
                     others in its peer group, the company may be considered undervalued and, therefore, a buying
                     opportunity. It may also be a sign that investors expect the company’s sales to increase in the
                     future. Like all other metrics, EV/sales should not be used on its own to value a company.

                               EXAMPLE
                               XYZ Inc.’s EV/sales ratio is $5,823,915 ÷ $810,192 = 7.19.



                     LO 3.b    Recognize compound annual growth rate (CAGR).
                                                                                                                                    Unit 3

                     Compound Annual Growth Rate (CAGR)
                     The compound annual growth rate (CAGR) is the rate at which an investment would grow if
                     it grew at a steady rate. Mathematically, CAGR is represented as:
                                                             1
                                    ending value  # of years
                          CAGR =                           −1
                                    beginning value 


                               EXAMPLE
                               An investment had the following returns:

                                          Date            Value
                                       1 Jan 20X1        $1,000
                                       1 Jan 20X2        $1,500
                                       1 Jan 20X3        $1,200
                                       1 Jan 20X4        $1,600
                                       1 Jan 20X5        $1,800
                               The CAGR of this investment would be:
                                             1
                                     $1, 800  4
                                                − 1 = 15.83%
                                      $1, 000 




S79_LEM_5E.indb 55                                                                                                        9/1/2020 1:51:55 PM
          56         Unit 3   Company Valuations



                                                   TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                   Calculating a CAGR with the calculators provided at the testing center would be
                                                   very difficult. The best way to calculate it is to test the four possible answers for
                                                   correctness. If the previous example was given in the exam, it may have the following
                                                   as listed as possible answers:
                                                          A. 10.78%
                                                          B. 12.50%
                                                          C. 15.83%
                                                          D. 20.00%
                                                   The answer can be determined by multiplying the beginning value ($1,000) by
                                                   (1 + each answer)number of years (4) and identifying which answer gives the ending value
                                                   ($1,800). In this case, the answer is 15.83%, or choice C.

                                                   EXAMPLE
                                                   An investment had the following returns:

                                                              Date           Value
                                                           1 Jan 20X1        $2,000
                                                           1 Jan 20X2        $1,500
                                                           1 Jan 20X3        $1,800
                                                           1 Jan 20X4        $2,200
                                                   What is the CAGR of this investment?
                                                        A. 1.00%
                                                        B. 3.22%
                                                        C. 5.25%
                                                        D. 10.00%
                                                   Answer B: The answer can be found by determining which growth rate results in an
                                                   ending value of $2,200.
                                                       $2,000 × (1.0100)3 = $2,060
                                                       $2,000 × (1.0322)3 = $2,199
                                                       $2,000 × (1.0525)3 = $2,332
                                                       $2,000 × (1.1000)3 = $2,662
                                                   Using our calculations, the answer can be identified as B., 3.22%.


                                      Dividend Payout Ratio

                                      LO 3.c       Calculate dividend payout ratio and yield.

                                      The dividend payout ratio is a percentage of net income used to pay dividends. It is calculated
                                      as:

                                                                         annual dividends paid
                                            dividend payout ratio =
                                                                             net income

                                      High-growth companies tend to have low dividend payout ratios because most of their net
                                      income goes to retained earnings, where it is used to fund future projects. More mature, stable
                                      companies will have higher dividend payout ratios.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 56                                                                                                                            9/1/2020 1:52:06 PM
                                                                                         Unit 3    Company Valuations     57


                               EXAMPLE
                               The dividend payout ratio for XYZ in 20X2 is $10,000 ÷ $200,000 = 5%, indicating
                               that 5% of all net income is paid out to common shareholders. Note the $10,000 in
                               dividend payments was found on the statement of cash flows.


                     Dividend Yield
                     Dividend yield is the annual dividends paid divided by the price per share, or:

                                             annual dividends paid
                          dividend yield =
                                                price per share

                     The dividend yield is the return an investor receives from an equity investment through
                     dividends. If the shares are held indefinitely, and the dividend stays constant, the dividend
                     yield is the annual return on the investment.

                               EXAMPLE
                               XYZ Inc.’s dividend yield is ($10,000 ÷ 600,000) ÷ $9.53 = 0.17%.
                               Remember to always compare like terms. Either the dividends need to be represented
                               on a per share basis (as demonstrated) or the price must be substituted for the market
                               capitalization.


                     Equity Value
                                                                                                                                  Unit 3
                     LO 3.d    Define equity value.


                     Many metrics used in determining equity value use price as the numerator. All these metrics
                     are identifying how much of a premium (or discount) is being paid for each dollar of the
                     denominator (e.g., EPS).


                     Price-to-Earnings (P/E)
                     One of the most frequently used measures for a company’s equity value is price-to-earnings, or
                     P/E. P/E is defined as:

                                     current price
                          P/E =
                                  earnings per share

                     This metric illustrates how much an investor is willing to pay for each dollar of net income
                     (on a per-share basis). P/E ratio values differ greatly between sectors. While a stable utility
                     company may have a low P/E ratio, high-growth companies, such as tech stocks, tend to have
                     very high P/E ratios. P/E ratios tend to be represented in two ways: trailing P/E and forward
                     P/E.

                               EXAMPLE
                               The LTM P/E for XYZ, Inc., is $9.53 ÷ ($200,000 ÷ 600,000) = 28.59.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 57                                                                                                      9/1/2020 1:52:21 PM
          58         Unit 3   Company Valuations



                                      Trailing (LTM) P/E Multiple
                                      Trailing P/E uses the earnings over the last 12 months to calculate EPS in the denominator.
                                      This is a conservative approach because the earnings over the last 12 months have been
                                      established, and no estimation is needed.


                                      Forward P/E Multiple
                                      Forward P/E multiples use estimates on what the EPS will be over the next 12 months. This
                                      requires more analysis to determine what the earnings will be in the future and is, therefore,
                                      more likely to have errors skewing the results.


                                      Price to Cash Flow (P/CF)
                                      Price to cash flow (P/CF) is:

                                                                     price / share
                                            P/CF =
                                                       operating cash flow / shares outstanding

                                      P/CF has the advantage over P/E; it is comparable between companies without adjustment
                                      because it does not include noncash expenses such as depreciation and amortization. It
                                      measures the amount an investor is willing to pay for each dollar of operating cash flow.

                                                   EXAMPLE
                                                   The P/CF for XYZ, Inc., is $9.53 ÷ ($240,000 ÷ 600,000) = 23.83.


                                      Price to Free Cash Flow
                                      A subset of P/CF is price to free cash flow (P/FCF). P/FCF works the same way as P/CF,
                                      except instead of using operating cash flows, free cash flows (operating cash flow less capital
                                      expenditures) are used. This is a more conservative method of valuing equity by cash flow.


                                      Price to Book Value
                                      Price to book value (P/BV) is a measure of how much each dollar of book value per share
                                      is worth. Book value is the stockholder’s equity portion of the balance sheet and can be
                                      calculated as assets less liabilities. Thus, P/BV is:
                                                                  price / share
                                            P/B =
                                                     stockholder equity / shares outstanding

                                      Companies that have low P/B ratios may be considered undervalued.

                                                   EXAMPLE
                                                   The P/BV for XYZ, Inc., is $9.53 ÷ ($3,434,900 ÷ 600,000) = 1.66.

                                                   TAKE NOTE
                                                   Occasionally, price to book value is stated as price to tangible book value. Tangible
                                                   book value is book value excluding intangible assets such as goodwill and patents.




S79_LEM_U3.indd 58                                                                                                                         9/3/2020 1:21:03 PM
                                                                                           Unit 3   Company Valuations   59


                     Price to Sales (P/S)
                     Price to sales (P/S) is a ratio that relates the value of a stock with its annual sales by dividing
                     its price per share by annual sales per share. P/S is generally of limited use for most companies
                     because it does not take into account any of the costs of doing business; however, it may be
                     useful in comparing companies that have very low profit margins or are operating at a loss.
                     Like other valuation metrics, P/S should only be used to compare companies in the same peer
                     group because norms can be significantly different between peer groups.

                                EXAMPLE
                                The P/S for XYZ, Inc., is $9.53 ÷ ($810,192 ÷ 600,000) = 7.06.


                     P/E to Growth (PEG)
                     The P/E to growth (PEG) ratio is a measure of a firm’s P/E while taking into account its
                     growth. PEG is calculated as:

                                               P/E
                          ​PEG ration = ___________
                                        ​   ​​
                                        annual EPS growth

                                TAKE NOTE
                                When calculating PEG, make sure to use the annual growth rate in terms of percentage
                                and not in decimal form. For example, if a company’s P/E was 15x and its growth rate
                                was 6%, the PEG would be 15 ÷ 6 = 2.5.

                                                                                                                                 Unit 3
                     PEG is a useful valuation metric because it allows for a more dynamic model where companies
                     grow. An ideally priced stock is going to have its P/E grow at the same rate as the EPS growth
                     rate. Thus, the benchmark value for PEG is one. Stocks that are over one may be overvalued
                     for their growth rate, while stock under one may be undervalued for their growth rate.

                                EXAMPLE
                                A company has a P/E of 5x, a PEG of 12.5, and a current EPS of $3. What is the expected
                                EPS for the following year?
                                Answer: First find what the EPS growth rate is by rewriting the PEG formula:

                                           P/E _      5
                                    g =_
                                       ​​      ​ = ​     ​ = 0.40%​​​
                                       PEG 12.5

                                Because EPS is expected to grow by 0.40%, multiply the current EPS by 1.004 to
                                calculate the next year’s EPS: 3 × 1.004 = 3.012.


                     LO 3.e     Identify normalized earnings.



                     Normalized Earnings
                     Many companies have cyclical earnings seasons. For example, many U.S. retailers tend to have
                     higher sales in the fourth quarter because of the holiday season. When making computations,
                     earnings will generally be normalized, or adjusted, to account for these cyclical changes. In
                     the previous retail example, the company’s earnings would be normalized by adjusting the first
                     three quarters up and the fourth quarter down to smooth out the cyclical effect.




S79_LEM_U3.indd 59                                                                                                        23/05/22 1:15 PM
          60         Unit 3   Company Valuations



                                      Earnings may also be normalized by removing extraordinary items. For example, a company
                                      may sell a factory, an event that occurs infrequently. Analysts should remove this unusual
                                      sale from earnings to normalize the company’s earnings; otherwise, the earnings would be
                                      overstated.


                                      LO 3.f       Define sum of the parts analysis.



                                      Sum of the Parts Analysis
                                      A corporation may be operating in several different business segments. When valuating these
                                      companies, it may be useful to analyze each business segment separately and sum the parts
                                      together for the value of the entire firm.

                                                   TAKE NOTE
                                                   The sum-of-the-parts assessment evaluates the different segments of a company. The
                                                   total value of all the segments would be the break-up value of the company.
                                      As an example, MichelleCo is a holding company that has the following operating subsidiaries
                                      and results.

                                             MPM        MichelleCo Print Media (newspapers and magazines)
                                             MTM        MichelleCo TV Media (TV stations/cable systems)
                                             MP         MichelleCo Publishing (textbooks)
                                             ME         MichelleCo Education (training/exam prep)

                                                               Earnings           Industry Peer P/E         Possible Valuation
                                             MPM               $5 million                   7                    $35 million
                                             MTM              $10 million                   10                 $100 million
                                             MP               $10 million                   12                 $120 million
                                             ME               $60 million                   20                   $1.2 billion
                                             Total                                                            $1.455 billion

                                      If MichelleCo has a market capitalization of less than $1.455 billion, it may be considered
                                      undervalued. This technique is especially useful when an investment banker is looking to
                                      either purchase or sell a business unit from a parent company.

             4                           Capital Budgeting




          LEARNING OBJECTIVES
                     When you have completed this unit, you will be able to accomplish the following.
                     ff LO 4.a   Interpret discounted cash flows.
                     ff LO 4.b   Compute cost of capital.
                     ff LO 4.c   Define economic profit (loss).
                     ff LO 4.d   Recall regulatory filings.
                     ff LO 4.e   Examine communications.


          INTRODUCTION
                     Capital budgeting, also called investment appraisal, is the process of determining whether a
                     long-term venture should be undertaken. The capital budgeting process primarily involves
                     valuating potential investments, deciding whether or not an investment should be made, and
                     determining the method and cost of financing.


          LESSON 4.1: CAPITAL BUDGETING ITEMS
                     LO 4.a      Interpret discounted cash flows.



                     Discounted Cash Flows
                     The most critical assumption used in capital budgeting analysis is that there is a time value
                     of money. In other words, cash is more valuable today than the same amount of cash in the
                     future. This time value is expressed as the discount rate or the interest rate funds could earn if
                     unused. This discount rate is the opportunity cost of a capital expenditure. Therefore, in order
                     for the expenditure to be worth undertaking, its return must be greater than the discount rate.
                                                                                                                          63



S79_LEM_5E.indb 63                                                                                                   9/1/2020 1:52:54 PM
          64         Unit 4   Capital Budgeting



                                       Discounted cash flows is the method whereby we apply the discount rate to different cash
                                       flows to make meaningful comparisons. Utilizing the discount rate, we can find how much
                                       today’s money would be worth in the future (the future value). Even more importantly, we
                                       can also find the reverse: how much money from future periods would be worth in today’s
                                       money (the present value).


                                       Future Value
                                       The future value of an asset is its current (or present) value plus the compounded interest that
                                       would be earned at the discount rate. Mathematically, this is represented as:

                                             FVt = PV(1 + i )t

                                             where:
                                             FV = future value
                                             PV = present value
                                             i = interest rate
                                             t = number of compounding periods in the future

                                                  EXAMPLE
                                                  If you currently have $100 and you can earn 3% compounded quarterly in a money
                                                  market fund, the value of your holding in one year is:
                                                  FV4 = $100 (1 + 0.03)4 = $112.55


                                       Present Value
                                       Present value is the opposite of future value. Because funds in the future are worth less than
                                       funds today, the future value needs to be discounted back to the present value. This is most
                                       commonly seen when we have a series of future cash flows. To find how much they are worth
                                       now, each cash flow needs to be discounted back to its present value. This concept will be seen
                                       in net present value computations. Present value is calculated as:
                                                     FVt
                                             PV =
                                                    (1 + i)t
                                             where:
                                             PV = present value
                                             FV = future value
                                             i = interest rate
                                             t = number of compounding periods in the future

                                                  EXAMPLE
                                                  If you want to have $100 in three years, and you can earn 5% compounded annually
                                                  in a money market fund, the amount you would need to put into the money market
                                                  account today is:

                                                                 $100
                                                       PV =                 = $86.38
                                                               (1 + .05)
                                                                        3




S79_LEM_5E.indb 64                                                                                                                   9/1/2020 1:53:17 PM
                                                                                            Unit 4   Capital Budgeting     65


                     Net Present Value
                     The net present value (NPV) is the sum of the present value of all-cash inflows (outflows).
                     Mathematically, NPV can be expressed either as:
                                              CF1      CF2              CFn
                           NPV = CF0 +              +       2
                                                              + ... +
                                             (1 + i) (1 + i)          (1 + i)n

                     or alternatively as:
                                     N
                                            CFt
                           NPV = ∑              t
                                    t=0 (t + i )

                          where:
                          NPV = net present value
                          CF0 = initial cash outflow (inflow)
                          CFt = cash flow at period t
                          i    = discount rate
                     NPV is one of the tools a company can use in determining whether or not to engage in an
                     investment. If the NPV is greater than zero, the project will increase the company’s worth. If
                     it is less than zero, the company will lose value. If the NPV is zero, the company is indifferent
                     to entering into the project.

                                EXAMPLE
                                A company is considering purchasing a new piece of machinery that costs $1,000,000.
                                It has an expected life of three years and will generate $500,000 each year. The
                                discount rate for the company is 5% compounded annually. The NPV for this project is:

                                                            500, 000   500, 000           500,000
                                     NPV = −1, 000, 000 +            +           + ... +            = 361, 624
                                                            (1 + .05) (1 + .05)2
                                                                                         (1 + .05)
                                                                                                  3



                     Because the NPV is greater than zero, the company should purchase the machine.

                                TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                Be sure to always have the symbols correct in your calculation. Always treat outflows
                                as negatives and inflows as positives.                                                             Unit 4

                     Internal Rate of Return (IRR)
                     The internal rate of return (IRR) of a project is the discount rate of a series of cash flows
                     where the NPV is equal to zero. It is a measure of what the rate of return on a project, or
                     investment, would be. When IRR is used to determine whether or not to proceed with a
                     project, the project should only be accepted if the IRR is greater than the discount rate.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 65                                                                                                       9/1/2020 1:53:41 PM
          66         Unit 4   Capital Budgeting



          Figure 4.1: IRR
                                                                 50
                                                                 40




                                             Net Present Value
                                                                 30
                                                                 20
                                                                                         IRR
                                                                 10
                                                                  0
                                                                 –10
                                                                 –20
                                                                 –30

                                                                         1   2   3   4   5   6   7   8   9

                                                                                 Discount Rate

                                       IRR should not generally be used to compare two mutually exclusive projects. IRR does not
                                       take into account the amount of initial capital needed, and, therefore, may exceed the project’s
                                       budget. Problems can also occur when there is more than one change in the sign of cash flows
                                       (e.g., when there is an outflow, an inflow, and the second outflow). In such cases, there may
                                       not be an IRR, or there may be multiple IRRs. As a general rule, IRR should only be used to
                                       decide whether to accept or reject a project or investment.

                                                                 TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                                 It is very difficult and time consuming to calculate an IRR without a financial
                                                                 calculator. You will not be asked to calculate it on the exam, but you are required to
                                                                 know the conceptual framework behind it.


                                       LO 4.b                    Compute cost of capital.



                                       Cost of Capital
                                       Not all capital can be raised at the same rate. There are a multitude of factors that differentiate
                                       the rate at which a company can raise funds, such as liquidity of investments and liquidation
                                       seniority. The three components of a firm’s capitalization are debt, preferred stock, and
                                       common stock.


                                       Cost of Common Stock
                                       There are many ways in which analysts determine the price and required return for common
                                       equity. However, two that tend to be used more than others are the capital asset pricing model
                                       (CAPM) and the dividend discount model (DDM).




S79_LEM_5E.indb 66                                                                                                                                        9/1/2020 1:53:42 PM
                                                                                            Unit 4   Capital Budgeting    67


                     Capital Asset Pricing Model (CAPM)
                     The CAPM is derived from Harry Markowitz’s introduction of modern portfolio theory in
                     1952, where assets can be valued solely on their relationship to the market portfolio (often
                     substituted with an index, such as the S&P 500). The CAPM is most frequently expressed as:

                           RE = RF + β(RM − RF)

                          where:
                          RE = required return on common equity
                          RF = risk-free rate
                          RM = return on a market portfolio
                          b = a measure of the sensitivity of an asset’s returns compared to excess market returns
                                (market returns less the risk-free rate)
                     The risk-free rate of return is usually tied to very high-quality short-term debt, such as the
                     six-month T-bill rate. Market returns are generally estimated by using a large market index
                     such as the S&P 500. Beta (b) is the variable most open to the differences in opinion in the
                     CAPM. Calculating beta involves a significant amount of statistical analysis. It is sufficient to
                     understand that if b < 1, the asset’s price will move less than the market; if b > 1, its price will
                     move more than the market, and if b = 1, its price will move in the same proportions as the
                     market.

                                  EXAMPLE
                                  If a company has a beta of 2, the S&P 500 is returning 5%, and the 90-day T-bill is
                                  yielding 2.5%, the required return on company’s equity is 2.5% + 2 × (5% – 2.5%) =
                                  7.5%.
                                  Remember that any time a beta is greater than one, the return must be larger than the
                                  market rate of return.

                                  TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                  Industry groups tend to have the same betas. Stable companies, such as utilities, often
                                  have betas less than one. Growth companies, such as new retail companies, generally
                                  have high betas.


                     Dividend Discount Model (DDM)
                                                                                                                                   Unit 4
                     For mature companies that consistently pay a dividend, it may be appropriate to use the
                     DDM to value a company. The DDM is defined as:
                                       ​D​1​​
                          ​​P​  0​​ = __ ​
                                      k−g
                          where:
                          P0 = current price of the stock
                          D1 = next year’s dividend
                          k = required rate of return for the stock
                          g = expected constant growth rate

                     The DDM simply treats common stock as a perpetuity, with the future cash flows being the
                     dividends, discounted by the returns over the constant growth (k – g).




S79_LEM_U4.indd 67                                                                                                          23/05/22 1:20 PM
          68         Unit 4   Capital Budgeting



                                       When using the DDM, the next period’s dividend is always used. If the only information
                                       available is the currents period’s dividend (D0), then multiply it by (1 + g) to grow the current
                                       dividend to the next period’s dividend.

                                                   TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                   Pay close attention to the dividends. Be certain they are the annual dividends, and
                                                   make sure you know which dividend you are using in the DDM. The question may
                                                   give either the current or next period’s dividend. If it is the current period, you need to
                                                   convert it to the next period’s dividend.

                                                   EXAMPLE
                                                   XYZ Corp. is paying a $0.12 dividend this year to common stockholders. Their
                                                   dividends have grown at a constant rate of 3% every year, and no change is expected.
                                                   The CAPM indicates that investors require a 5% return on XYZ’s common stock.
                                                   According to the DDM, the market price for XYZ common stock is ($0.12 × 1.03) ÷ (0.05 –
                                                   0.03) = $6.18.
                                                   Note that the dividend supplied needed to first be converted to next period’s divided
                                                   for use in the DDM formula.
                                       In a formula with four variables, you can solve for any one if you are given the other three.
                                       The DDM, therefore, can be used not only to find the stock price, but also the required
                                       return, if the market price of the stock is given. When using the DDM to find the rate of
                                       return for common equity, it can be rewritten:

                                                   D1
                                              k=      +g
                                                   P0

                                       As long as you remember one way of writing the DDM, using algebra will allow you to
                                       rewrite the equation to find any of the four variables.

                                                   EXAMPLE
                                                   LMN, Inc., is a mature company with stable earnings and dividend growth. It currently
                                                   is paying a $0.05 quarterly dividend, and it has a growth rate of 2%. LMN’s current
                                                   market price is 15.25. The required rate of return on LMN common stock is ($0.20 ×
                                                   1.02) ÷ $15.25 + 0.02 = 3.34%.
                                                   Remember that the DDM requires the use of annual dividends, so the $0.05 quarterly
                                                   dividend needed to be converted by multiplying it by 4. Additionally, the dividend
                                                   needed to be adjusted to the next year’s dividend.


                                       Cost of Preferred Stock
                                       Preferred stock has the simplest way of measuring its required return of the three
                                       capitalization components. The method of computing the required return on preferred stock
                                       is very similar to that of a perpetuity because they share characteristics. Like perpetuities,
                                       preferred stock is generally purchased and held for an indefinite period, using only its regular
                                       cash flows (dividends) as investment income. Therefore, to calculate the required return, one




S79_LEM_5E.indb 68                                                                                                                               9/1/2020 1:54:14 PM
                                                                                          Unit 4    Capital Budgeting      69


                     must find the discount rate at which the price is the present value of all future cash flows. This
                     can be simply written as:
                                                      annual preferred dividends
                          cost of preferred stock =
                                                        price of preferred stock

                     Because preferred stock constitutes equity ownership in a company, preferred dividends are
                     not tax exempt like bond interest, so no tax adjustments are necessary when calculating cash
                     flows from its dividends.

                                EXAMPLE
                                ABC Co. has 1 million shares of preferred stock outstanding. They are currently trading
                                for $54.25 and pay a semiannual dividend of $1.50. What is the required return for
                                ABC’s preferred stock?
                                Answer:

                                     ($1.50× 2)
                                                  = 5.53%
                                       $54.25


                     Cost of Debt
                     The cost of debt financing is intuitively the easiest of all capital costs. When debt is issued,
                     attached is a coupon rate, which the company will pay over the life of the obligation.
                     Therefore, the coupon rate is the cost of debt. Algebraically, it is:
                                            interest expense
                          cost of debt =
                                           principal required

                     Unlike equity, where the cost of raising capital is expensed, the expense of raising debt needs
                     to be included in the cost of capital. Therefore, the true cost of debt is not as simple as just the
                     coupon because the principal received is not the same as the principal issued. Thus, the cost of
                     debt will increase with the decrease in principle received.

                                EXAMPLE

                                                                                                                                  Unit 4
                                XYZ Partners issued $10,000,000 of 8% debentures to fund a new warehouse. The
                                underwriters are charging a 3% spread as issuance costs. What is the cost of capital for
                                XYZ?
                                Answer: XYZ will have to annually pay $800,000 in interest expense. Because there is
                                a 3% spread for issuance costs, the principal XYZ receives is therefore $10,000,000 –
                                $300,000 = $9,700,000. The pretax cost of debt is $800,000 ÷ $9,700,000 = 8.25%.
                     Interest, unlike dividends, receives preferential tax treatment. Because it is deducted before
                     the computation of the firm’s tax expense, the amount saved needs to be included in the
                     computation of the cost of debt financing as well. The after-tax cost of debt is calculated as:

                                                      interest expense
                          after-tax cost of debt =                      ×(1 − marginal tax rate )
                                                     principal received

                     The after-tax cost of debt will, with the exception of a zero tax rate, always be lower than the
                     pretax cost of debt, thanks to the tax deduction from interest, and is the effective cost of debt
                     to the firm.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 69                                                                                                      9/1/2020 1:54:47 PM
          70         Unit 4   Capital Budgeting



                                       Weighted Average Cost of Capital (WACC)
                                       Companies rarely choose to fund their operations through only one form of capital. More
                                       typically, it is a combination of two or three factors of capitalization. When computing the
                                       discount rate, or cost of capital, for a project, it is vital to take the differing costs for each
                                       component into account. This is done through computing the weighted average cost of
                                       capital (WACC). The WACC is simply the sum of the after-tax cost of each component of
                                       capitalization multiplied by its weight in total capitalization, more formally expressed as:

                                              WACC = w d R d (1 − tax rate) + w p R p + w E R E

                                       When computing the WACC, it is easiest to compute it using a table like the following.

          Figure 4.2: WACC for XYZ Corp. (marginal tax rate of 35%)

           Component                Weight          Pretax Cost       After-Tax Cost      Weighted Cost
           Debt                       35%               7.5%               4.875%               1.71%
           Preferred                  15%              10%                10%                   1.50%
           Common                     50%              15%                15%                   7.50%
           Total                    100%                                                       10.71%


                                                  TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                  It is easy for students to forget to use the after-tax cost of debt when computing the
                                                  WACC. Always scan the question to see if the cost of debt given is pretax or after-tax.

                                                  TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                  The weight for each component of capital is not always given to you. To find what the
                                                  weight is for each component of capital, take the amount raised by that component
                                                  (e.g., how much funding is coming from common stock) and divide it by the total
                                                  amount of capital raised.

                                                  EXAMPLE
                                                  A firm is raising $15,000,000 for a new factory. It will issue $5,000,000 in bonds,
                                                  $2,500,000 in preferred stock, and 500,000 shares of common stock with a price of $15.
                                                  What is the weight of each component of capital?
                                                  The firm is raising a total of $15,000,000 for its project, so each component will be
                                                  a percentage of $15,000,000. The debt raised is given as $5,000,000, so debt has a
                                                  weight of 33.33% of funds raised. Preferred stock, likewise, is given in the problem
                                                  ($2,500,000). It is, therefore, 2,500,000 ÷ 15,000,000 = 16.67%. Equity is not directly
                                                  given. You can compute the amount raised either through multiplying the number
                                                  of shares by the price per share or by subtracting the debt and preferred stock
                                                  components from the total capital. Equity is therefore 500,000 × $15 = $7,500,000,
                                                  resulting in a capital weight of 50%.

                                                  TAKE NOTE
                                                  A company’s WACC can be used as the discount rate when computing present value,
                                                  future value, or NPV.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 70                                                                                                                          9/1/2020 1:54:59 PM
                                                                                            Unit 4   Capital Budgeting        71



                     LO 4.c    Define economic profit (loss).



                     Economic Profit (Loss)
                     An important consideration to make before starting any project is the differences between
                     accounting profit and economic profit. Using just accounting profit, any project that will
                     result in revenues greater than expenses should be undertaken. However, this does not account
                     for the opportunity cost of the assets used. The opportunity cost is the returns foregone by
                     using the assets in the project. Economic profit accounts for both explicit and implicit costs
                     involved in a project. If a project has economic profit, it will add value to the company. If a
                     project has economic loss, it will reduce value from the company.

                               TAKE NOTE
                               A company is thinking about hiring a temporary employee for one year. The
                               employee’s wage for the year would be $30,000, and he would produce $32,000
                               worth of service. The company also is invested in a fund that will yield 7% over the
                               year. While the company has made an economic profit of $2,000 from hiring the new
                               employee, it has made an economic loss of $100 because the company could have
                               made $32,100 from investing the $30,000. Therefore, the company should not hire the
                               temporary employee.

                               KNOWLEDGE CHECK
                               1.   When computing the discount rate for a project, it is vital to take the differing costs

                                    apply?
                                     A. The coupon
                                     B. The maturity date
                                     C. The interest payment dates
                                     D. Issue price

                               Answers
                               1. A When computing the discount rate, or cost of capital, for a project, it is vital to

                                    component of capitaliza­tion multiplied by its weight in total capitalization. LO 4.b

                               2. C An important consideration to make before starting any project is the differences
                                    between accounting profit and economic profit. Using just accounting profit, any
                                    project that will result in revenues greater than expenses should be undertaken.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 71                                                                                                       9/1/2020 1:54:59 PM

                                                        However, this does not account for the opportunity cost of the assets used. If a

          LESSON 4.2: WHERE TO GET DATA

                                       LO 4.d     Recall regulatory filings.

                                       To perform their duties, investment bankers need to analyze trends in the market, specific
                                       industry sectors, individual companies, and comparable companies. To makes these analyses,
                                       investment bankers require access to a wide range of data. This data includes financial,
                                       performance, issuance, and transaction data that may be retrieved from various commercial
                                       and proprietary market databases, regulatory sources, internet sites, media, and other
                                       resources. While there may be a wide range of data sources, the difficult task is sorting
                                       through it and finding resources that provide high quality, relevant information.


                                       Regulatory Filings
                                       The Securities Exchange Act of 1934 requires publicly held companies to make certain
                                       regulatory filings to the SEC. These filings are available for free on the SEC’s Electronic Data
                                       Gathering Analysis and Retrieval (EDGAR) database and allow the public to gain easier access
                                       to reports on the company’s financial and operating condition. While many of these reports
                                       are made on a regular basis (e.g., annually or quarterly), several of the reports are also only
                                       filed with the SEC as needed (e.g., the current report).


                                       Issuer Filings

                                       Annual Report (Form 10-K)
                                       Reporting companies are required to file an audited, annual report with the SEC. This report,
                                       released no more than 90 days after the firm’s fiscal year, contains a variety of information that
                                       is useful for an investment banker. Included are
                                        a description of the firm’s business;
                                        audited financial statements;
                                        executive compensation;
                                        description of firm operations, market conditions, and key competitors; and
                                        any legal proceedings.


                                       Quarterly Report (Form 10-Q)
                                       Each reporting firm must file a quarterly report at the end of the first three fiscal quarters (the
                                       year-end report is filed on Form 10-K). The Form 10-Q contains similar information as the
                                       Form 10-K, but it is less detailed. The financial statements provided therein are not required
                                       to be audited.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 72                                                                                                                          9/1/2020 1:55:00 PM
                                                                                          Unit 4   Capital Budgeting       73


                                TAKE NOTE
                                The SEC previously had a separate form for quarterly reports of small businesses,
                                called the Form 10-QSB.

                                TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                Quarterly reports must be filed with the SEC within 45 days of the quarter’s end, or the
                                exchange can suspend their trading, due to a reporting deficiency.


                     Current Reports (Form 8-K)
                     A current report (Form 8-K) is required to be filed whenever there is a material change in the
                     company that may be of interest to either shareholders or the SEC. Such events may include
                      bankruptcy,
                      change in accountant,
                      an unregistered resale of equity securities,
                      change in the code of ethics, and
                      delisting from an exchange.
                     Current reports are required to be filed not later than four business days after the material
                     event occurs.


                     Transition Report
                     Any time an issuer changes their fiscal year, a transition report must be filed with the SEC. A
                     transition report contains the audited financial information for the issuer covering the period
                     between the closing date of the previous fiscal year and the start of the new fiscal year. The
                     issuer, however, still maintains an obligation to file an annual report for any fiscal year ending
                     before the changing of fiscal years. A transition report may never cover a period of 12 months
                     or more.

                     If the period covered in the transition report is less than six months, it may be filed on a
                     Form 10-Q, otherwise it must be filed on an annual report. If the transition report is filed on
                     a Form 10-Q, the financial statements do not need to be audited. The issuer will then have
                     to include the audited financial statements of the transition period separately with their next
                                                                                                                                 Unit 4
                     annual report.


                     Schedules 13D and 13G
                     Schedule 13D, or the beneficial ownership report, is filed whenever either an individual or a
                     group acquire more than 5% of the voting shares of a company’s stock. Schedule 13D must
                     be filed with the SEC within 10 days of the acquisition. Additionally, the schedule must be
                     provided to the company and each securities exchange where the security is traded.

                     An investor may make use of Schedule 13G—a short form of Schedule 13D—if it meets
                     certain conditions. To file Schedule 13G, an investor must acquire less than 20% of the
                     company’s voting shares, also making it clear that the investment is passive and that no
                     control will be exerted over the company’s management.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 73                                                                                                     9/1/2020 1:55:00 PM
          74         Unit 4   Capital Budgeting



                                       Form 13F
                                       Each quarter, investment managers who have investment discretion over $100 million
                                       or more are required to file with the SEC Form 13F. This form is a report of all the 13(f )
                                       securities the manager holds. Included in 13(f ) securities are all stocks listed on an exchange,
                                       Nasdaq stocks, warrants, equity options, shares in closed-end investment companies, and
                                       certain convertible debt securities. Form 13F must be filed within 45 days of the end of a
                                       calendar quarter.

                                                  TAKE NOTE
                                                  Shares in an open-end investment company do not qualify as 13(f) securities.


                                       Proxy Statements (Form 14A)
                                       Prior to any action that requires a vote from the shareholders, reporting companies must file a
                                       proxy statement (Form 14A). Proxy statements should contain sufficient information to allow
                                       shareholders to make an informed decision. Generally, proxy statements are sent before the
                                       company’s annual meeting, allowing shareholders to vote by proxy; however, they may occur
                                       more frequently when a shareholder vote cannot wait until the next annual meeting.


                                       Beneficial Ownership Reports (Forms 3, 4, and 5)
                                       Directors, officers, and beneficial owners of more than 10% of a company’s stock must make
                                       disclosure of their holdings in the company’s stock on Forms 3, 4, and 5. Initial disclosure is
                                       required within 10 days of becoming a director, officer, or 10% shareholder on a Form 3. Any
                                       changes in their holdings would then be reported within two days of the change on Form 4.
                                       Form 5 is only used when the filing of a Form 4 has been deferred.


                                       Communications With Clients and Other Departments

                                       LO 4.e     Examine communications.

                                       Some of the best information resources an investment banker has are those around herself.
                                       Others may have a deeper understanding into the client’s situation, have special knowledge
                                       about current market trends, and have data that is not available from a public repository, such
                                       as the EDGAR database. As a result, investment bankers need to be able to communicate
                                       with a wide range of people to ensure they have all the pertinent information to perform their
                                       duties.


                                       Communications With Clients
                                       Investment bankers need to have an open relationship with their clients. Frequently,
                                       investment bankers are required to complete filings, statements, and marketing materials for
                                       their clients and will need to have access to accurate and meaningful information. With this
                                       information, an investment banker will better be able to assist the client with issuing new
                                       shares or determining how to proceed with a merger or acquisition. Every investment banker
                                       needs to ensure they have a proper understanding of their client’s situation as part of their
                                       fiduciary responsibility to making sound advice.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 74                                                                                                                     9/1/2020 1:55:00 PM
                                                                                        Unit 4   Capital Budgeting      75


                     Communications With Industry Specialists
                     Investment bankers may need to consult with specialists in investment banking and capital
                     markets to obtain information regarding business opportunities. Recent trends in investment
                     banking activities can be a good indicator of how to proceed with a transaction. In addition,
                     these specialists may have access to industry data that will assist in determining what
                     marketing strategies are best suited for the company.


                     Communications With Research Department
                     A broker-dealer’s research department can be a tremendous source of information on specific
                     companies, sectors, and the markets in general. Occasionally, both a broker-dealer’s research
                     and investment banking department will be analyzing the same company. While the research
                     department may be a valuable resource to an investment banking department and vice versa,
                     procedures need to be put in place to avoid any potential conflicts of interest that may arise
                     from the two departments communicating with each other. These information barriers are
                     intended to protect the research department from viewing the material nonpublic information
                     that the investment banking department may be privy to and from pressure to release positive
                     research reports on an investment banking client.


                     Independence of Research Analysts
                     Research analysts may be subject to pressure from investment bankers to release research
                     reports that are favorable to investment banking clients instead of an unbiased opinion. As a
                     result, research analysts may not be supervised by any member of a firm’s investment banking
                     department. Additionally, investment bankers may not be on any committee that reviews,
                     evaluates, or decides compensation for research analysts.

                     As an additional safeguard for the independence of research analysts, member firms are
                     required to implement procedures that protect research analysts from retaliation, should they
                     issue an unfavorable research report. These safeguards must ensure members of the investment
                     banking department may not, directly or indirectly, take any action to retaliate against the
                     research analyst and that the firm does not discipline an analyst for the sole purpose of an
                     unfavorable research report.


                     Independence of Research Reports
                     Both FINRA and New York Stock Exchange (NYSE) rules require that only personnel in the                    Unit 4
                     research, legal, or compliance departments may approve research reports. At no time may a
                     member of the investment banking department have authority to approve or disapprove a
                     research report.

                     Research reports, however, may be reviewed by nonresearch personnel (e.g., investment
                     bankers) to verify the factual accuracy of the information or identify any potential conflict
                     of interest. Any communication between research and nonresearch personnel must be made
                     through or in the presence of a member of the legal or compliance department.

                               EXAMPLE
                               A broker-dealer’s research department covers XYZ Corp., one of its investment banking
                               customers. A research analyst is completing a report on XYZ Corp. but wants to have it
                               double checked by one of the investment bankers working with XYZ. The investment
                               banker may review the research report for factual errors but must communicate any of
                               his findings through a member of the compliance department.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 75                                                                                                   9/1/2020 1:55:00 PM
          76         Unit 4   Capital Budgeting



                                       Communications With the Syndicate Desk
                                       A broker-dealer’s syndicate desk is actively engaged in current offers. This gives them ready
                                       knowledge on the terms of deals that are in the marketplace, current market demands,
                                       security pricing, the structure of deals, and what covenants are being used in new issues. This
                                       information will greatly enable an investment banker to tailor any deal or offering to what the
                                       current market trends and norms are.


                                       Coordination With Other Departments
                                       Investment bankers may need to coordinate with other departments to review data for
                                       inclusion in marketing materials to ensure accuracy and permission to use the material.
                                       All marketing material needs to be sent to and approved by a principal, generally from the
                                       compliance department.

                                                  KNOWLEDGE CHECK
