            LESSON 7.6: DISTRIBUTIONS OF SECURITIES WITH CONFLICTS

                        FINRA Rule 5121

                        LO 7.o    Define an independent qualified underwriter.

                        When a member firm, for example, wishes to go public, it must abide by FINRA Rule 5121.
                        In the IPO, the member, to avoid a clear conflict of interest, must engage an independent
                        qualified underwriter to help it
                         perform due diligence and
                         prepare the registration statement and the prospectus.
                        The securities must have a bona fide public market or be investment grade.

                        FINRA Rule 5121 does not apply to the issuing member and its employees. These are
                        considered issuer-directed sales.


                        Independent Qualified Underwriter
                        An independent qualified underwriter must have been a book-running manager of at least
                        three public offerings of equity securities of which each must have been for not less than 50%
                        of the anticipated gross proceeds of the proposed offering for at least the three-year period
                        immediately preceding the filing of the registration statement.

                        No person associated with the firm in a supervisory capacity with respect to public offerings
                        may have been convicted, within the previous five years, of having violated the antifraud
                        provisions of federal securities law.




S79_LEM_U7.indd 139                                                                                                         24/05/22 11:02 PM
          140         Unit 7   The Registration Process



                                        Suitability
                                        In recommending the purchase of its employer’s stock to customers, registered representatives
                                        of the member going public must make certain that all buyers are suitable.

                                        Before an associated person makes a recommendation to any customer, there must be a
                                        reasonable basis for the recommendation. The suitability of the recommendation must
                                        consider income, net worth, investment objectives, risk tolerance, and other security
                                        positions. The registered options principal must make sure the options transactions are in line
                                        with the customer’s profile.

                                        There are three main obligations:
                                         Reasonable-basis suitability
                                         Customer-specific suitability
                                         Quantitative suitability
                                        Reasonable-basis obligation requires a member firm to have a reasonable basis to believe,
                                        having applied reasonable diligence, that a recommendation is suitable for an investor. What
                                        constitutes reasonable diligence varies depending on the complexity and risks associated
                                        with the security or investment strategy. Reasonable diligence must lead to recognizing the
                                        potential risks and rewards associated with the recommendation. The inability to distinguish
                                        and explain the risks when recommending a security or strategy violates the suitability rule.

                                        Customer-specific obligation requires a member to have a reasonable basis to believe that
                                        the recommendation is suitable for a particular customer based on that customer’s investment
                                        profile. The broker must attempt to obtain and analyze a broad array of customer-specific
                                        factors to support this determination.

                                        Quantitative suitability requires a member who has control over a customer account to have a
                                        reasonable basis for believing that a series of recommended transactions, even if suitable when
                                        viewed in isolation, are not excessive and unsuitable for the customer when taken together.
                                        No single test defines excessive activity; however, factors such as the turnover rate, the cost-
                                        equity ratio, and the use of in-and-out trading in a customer’s account may provide a basis for
                                        a finding that a member has violated the quantitative suitability obligation.

                                                     TAKE NOTE
                                                     Even if individual trades are suitable when viewed in isolation, the representative and
                                                     principal must make certain any series of transactions are suitable when viewed all
                                                     together. This higher-level view of an entire portfolio’s combined trades is known as
                                                     quantitative suitability.

                                                      TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                                     Records of each suitability determination must be maintained by the member for
                                                     three years.


                                        Regulation Best Interest
                                        Suitability has been the keystone to making recommendations to customers and clients
                                        through the modern era of securities business services. Regulation Best Interest (BI) is the
                                        21st-century answer to people being seen as individual investors and families with unique
                                        levels of risk tolerance and understanding. Simply having a customer risk profile questionnaire
                                        doesn’t suffice for risk alignment with a recommended security or strategy of a customer. It




S79_LEM_U7.indd 140                                                                                                                            24/05/22 11:02 PM
                                                                                    Unit 7   The Registration Process   141


                      is a challenge to prove that the adviser and client have the same definition of, for example,
                      moderately conservative in mind. Interviews with regulators are going to require serious and
                      disciplined thought processes. Outside, third-party help may be needed and is not at all
                      frowned upon by regulators.

                      When making a recommendation, a broker-dealer (or investment adviser) must act in the
                      retail customer’s best interest and cannot place its interests ahead of the customer’s interests,
                      known as the general obligation. The general obligation is satisfied only if the broker-dealer
                      complies with four specified component obligations.

                      The four key standards (obligations) under the new rule must apply; otherwise, the firm is not
                      in compliance.


                      1. Disclosure Obligation

                                                                                                                               Unit 7
                      The standard of conduct established by Regulation BI will not be satisfied through disclosure
                      alone. The standard of conduct draws from key principles underlying fiduciary obligations.
                      Importantly, the retail investor will be entitled to a recommendation from a broker-dealer that
                      is in the best interest of the retail investor, and that does not place the interests of the firm or
                      the financial professional ahead of the interests of the retail investor.

                                 EXAMPLE
                                 Retail customers that intend to buy and hold investments may find that paying a
                                 one-time commission to a broker-dealer recommending the investment is more
                                 cost-effective than paying an ongoing advisory fee. Retail customers with limited
                                 investment assets may benefit from broker-dealer recommendations when they do
                                 not meet minimum qualifications for advisory accounts imposed by some firms. Other
                                 retail customers who hold a variety of investments may benefit from having both
                                 brokerage and advisory accounts.


                      2. Care Obligation
                      Under this obligation, a broker-dealer, for example, must exercise reasonable diligence, care,
                      and skill when making a recommendation to a retail customer. The broker-dealer must
                      understand the potential risks, rewards, and costs associated with the recommendation.

                      Investment advisors are currently held to a fiduciary standard, but Reg BI introduces a whole
                      new level of care beyond suitability for broker-dealers. Suitability is no longer enough, as we
                      will see as follows.

                      If a recommended security is complex, the associated person making the recommendation
                      must be knowledgeable of that security’s characteristics. The broker-dealer must then consider
                      those risks, rewards, and costs in light of the customer’s investment profile and have a
                      reasonable basis to believe that the recommendation is in the customer’s best interest and does
                      not place the broker-dealer’s interest ahead of the retail customer’s interest. A broker-dealer
                      should consider reasonable alternatives, if any, in determining whether it has a reasonable
                      basis for making the recommendation.

                      Whether a broker-dealer has complied with the care obligation will be evaluated at the time of
                      the recommendation (and not in hindsight). When recommending a series of transactions, the
                      broker-dealer must have a reasonable basis to believe that the transactions taken together are
                      not excessive, even if each is in the customer’s best interest when viewed in isolation.




S79_LEM_U7.indd 141                                                                                                     24/05/22 11:02 PM
          142         Unit 7   The Registration Process



                                        3. Conflict of Interest Obligation
                                        A broker-dealer must establish, maintain, and enforce written policies and procedures
                                        addressing conflicts of interest associated with its recommendations to retail customers with
                                        the view to identify all such conflicts and, at a minimum, disclose or eliminate them.

                                                     EXAMPLE
                                                     If a broker-dealer offers only proprietary (or other limited range of products), the
                                                     policies and procedures must be reasonably designed to disclose the limitations and
                                                     associated conflicts and to prevent the limitations from causing the associated person
                                                     or broker-dealer from placing associated person’s or broker-dealer’s interests ahead of
                                                     the customer’s interest.
                                                     Firm policies and procedures must be created with the view to eliminate sales
                                                     contests, sales quotas, bonuses, and noncash compensation that are based on the
                                                     sale of securities within a limited period.


                                        4. Compliance Obligation
                                        A securities enterprise must have in place procedures designed with the view to achieving
                                        compliance with Regulation BI as a whole. Accordingly, your firm’s systems must address not
                                        only conflicts of interest but also compliance with its Disclosure and Care Obligations under
                                        Regulation BI. Firms are required to keep records as well.


                                        Discretionary Accounts
                                        With regard to discretionary accounts, registered representatives of the member going public
                                        cannot purchase shares for these accounts without first receiving the customer’s written
                                        permission.


                                        Affiliated Relationships
                                        The requirement to use an independent qualified underwriter applies not only to IPOs of the
                                        member’s shares but also to IPOs where there is an affiliated relationship between issuer and
                                        member. An affiliation exists if, between the two, more than 10% of the shares are owned.


                                        Escrow of Proceeds
                                        All proceeds from a public offering by a member firm of its own securities must be placed
                                        in escrow. The member firm must then perform a net capital computation, demonstrating
                                        the net capital of the firm is no less than 120% of the minimum or that the Aggregate
                                        Indebtedness to Net Capital (AI:NC) ratio is no greater than 10:1. In performing the
                                        calculation, the proceeds from the offering may be taken in consideration in calculating net
                                        capital.

                                                     EXAMPLE
                                                     A member is 100% owned by a privately held corporation. If the corporation wanted to
                                                     go public, the member could not act as syndicate manager. The member must engage
                                                     an independent qualified underwriter.




S79_LEM_U7.indd 142                                                                                                                            24/05/22 11:02 PM
                                                                                     Unit 7   The Registration Process   143


                                 TAKE NOTE
                                 All customer checks received to purchase the new issue must be placed in an escrow
                                 account pending a net capital computation.

                                 KNOWLEDGE CHECK
                                 1.   To be considered as an independent qualified underwriter the firm must
                                       A. have been approved as such no earlier than 30 days before the offering.
                                       B. have been a managing underwriter of at least three public offerings of equity
                                           securities.
                                       C. have run a book for not less than 60% of the anticipated gross proceeds of the
                                           proposed offering.
                                       D. for at least one year of the three-year period immediately preceding the
                                           filing of the registration statement have not participated as a manager of an
                                           underwriting.

                                 Answers                                                                                          Unit 7
                                 1. B An independent qualified underwriter must have been a book-running manager
                                      of at least three public offerings of equity securities of which each must have been
                                      for not less than 50% of the anticipated gross proceeds of the proposed offering for
                                      at least the three-year period immediately preceding the filing of the registration
                                      statement. LO 7.o


            LESSON 7.7: CORPORATE FINANCING DEPARTMENT

                       Rule 5110
                       LO 7.p    Define the offerings subject to FINRA Rule 5110.

                       The Corporate Financing Department (CFD), established by the Board of Governors of
                       FINRA, was created to review the fairness of compensation to be received by underwriters.
                       The offerings subject to review are equity and convertible debt issues.

                       Nonconvertible debt and nonconvertible preferred stock are exempt as long as they are rated
                       as investment grade. Also exempt from having to file are offerings under Regulation D, U.S.
                       government and agency securities, municipal securities, open-end management companies,
                       and unit trusts.

                       Closed-end funds (publicly traded funds) are subject to filing, as are Regulation A+ offerings,
                       Rule 147 offerings, DPPs, right offerings, and securities sold under FINRA Rule 5121.

                                 TAKE NOTE
                                 The CFD is controlled by the Board of Governors, which means the board can exempt
                                 any offering from having to file if it deems an exemption to be in the public interest.


                       Information Filed
                       To determine fairness, the syndicate manager files the following information with the CFD:
                        The underwriting spread
                        Reimbursement of expenses from issuer to underwriter




S79_LEM_U7.indd 143                                                                                                        24/05/22 11:02 PM
          144         Unit 7   The Registration Process



                                         The amount of any stock, warrants, or options received as part of the compensation
                                          package
                                         The right of first refusal to participate in future underwritings
                                         Nonaccountable expense allowances
                                         All other items of value, including information on overallotment
                                        The CFD will also review any items of compensation received by the underwriters during the
                                        180-day period immediately preceding the filing of the registration statement. The idea is to
                                        determine if any of these items have been received in connection with the offering. If received
                                        during the 180-day period preceding the filing, the CFD will presume the compensation was
                                        received in connection with the offering.

                                        The information previously noted must be filed with FINRA within one business day of filing
                                        a registration statement with the SEC.


                                        Unreasonable Compensation
                                        The CFD considers the following to be unreasonable compensation:
                                         Any options or warrants received by the underwriters that are exercisable below the public
                                          offering price (POP)
                                         Any options or warrants received by the underwriters that have a life of more than five
                                          years
                                         Any freely transferable stock received by the underwriters; stock received as part of the
                                          compensation package must be restricted for resale for 180 days from effective date; if the
                                          amount of securities received does not exceed 1% of the securities being offered, the lock-
                                          up period will not apply
                                         Any nonaccountable expense allowance in excess of 3% of the underwriting spread
                                         Any right of first refusal for future public or private offerings that has a duration of more
                                          than three years from the effective date
                                         Any overallotment option (green shoe clause) that allows the underwriters to purchase,
                                          from the issuer, more than 15% of the stock being offered for public resale

                                        Additionally, the CFD will do a pro forma exercise of any options or warrants received; if,
                                        upon exercise, the number of shares combined with any shares received up front exceeds 10%
                                        of the shares being offered to the public, this is considered unreasonable compensation.


                                        Reimbursement of Expenses
                                        In reviewing reimbursement of expenses from issuer to underwriter, the CFD looks for
                                        reimbursements that are normally borne by the underwriters. If the underwriters are
                                        reimbursed by the issuer for paying blue-sky fees, there is no problem because these fees are
                                        normally paid by the issuer. If, however, the underwriters are reimbursed for solicitation costs,
                                        this reimbursement would be considered part of the overall compensation package because
                                        this expense is normally borne by the underwriters and would be considered an unreasonable
                                        syndicate expense from the underwriting fee.

                                        If informed by the CFD that the compensation is unreasonable, the syndicate manager must
                                        immediately notify the other syndicate members and take steps to adjust the compensation
                                        items. Otherwise, the offering cannot occur.

                                                                          Unit 7   The Registration Process   145

          LESSON 7.8: REPORTING REQUIREMENTS AND INSIDER RULES

                                        The Act of 1934 Reports

                                        LO 7.q       Recall the Act of 1934 reporting requirements.



                                        Filings by Issuers
                                        Once an issuer is publicly traded, it is obligated to file certain reports with the SEC. These
                                        include an annual report on Form 10-K and quarterly reports on Form 10-Q.

                                        Form 8-K is another required report. This form is used to report newsworthy events to the
                                        SEC, such as change in management, change in the company’s name, mergers or acquisitions,
                                        and major new product introductions. This form is used only by domestic issuers. Foreign
                                        issuers are exempt. ADRs, which are U.S.-registered securities, are exempt because the
                                        underlying company is non-U.S.


                                        Filings by Nonissuers
                                        Corporate insiders, such as officers and directors, are required to file information on changes
                                        in their holdings with the SEC. Form 3 is used for the initial report, while Form 4 is used
                                        for subsequent reports. When an investor, investor group, or other entity acquires a 5% or
                                        more interest in an issuer of securities, that group must file Form 13-D with the SEC. The
                                        form must include information on the group’s intentions with regard to seeking control of the
                                        issuer. The form is also filed with the issuer and with the market where the security principally
                                        trades.

                                                     TAKE NOTE
                                                     Any changes in insider holdings must be reported to the SEC within two business days
                                                     of the change.
                                        Form 13-D filing requirements do not apply to insiders. If an officer acquires a 5% or more
                                        interest, this information is provided to the SEC on Form 4.

                                        Form 13-G is used by investment companies and related entities if they acquire a 5% or more
                                        interest in an issuer. Under the Investment Company Act of 1940, investment companies are
                                        prohibited from controlling or seeking to control any issuer. Therefore, a 13-G, not 13-D, is
                                        the required filing, and it must be made within 45 days after each calendar year-end during
                                        which the position is held.




S79_LEM_U7.indd 146                                                                                                                         24/05/22 11:02 PM
                                                                                      Unit 7   The Registration Process   147


                                 TEST TOPIC ALERT
                                 The following table highlights the timing requirements for various SEC filings.

                                  Form/Schedule          When Filed
                                         8K              Within four business days of an event of consequence
                                        10K              Within 90 days of year-end
                                        10Q              Within 45 days of the end of the quarter
                                        13D              Within 10 days of acquiring a 5% or more beneficial
                                                         ownership of an issuer
                                        13G              Within 45 days of the end of the calendar year after
                                                         acquiring a 5% or more beneficial ownership of an issuer
                                       Form 4            Within two business days of any change
                                       Form 3            Within 10 calendar days

                                                                                                                                 Unit 7
                                     Form 13F            Within 45 days of the end of the calendar quarter

                      Insiders are officers, directors, or 10% shareholders. In addition to monthly reporting of
                      changes in ownership, insiders are prohibited from the following:
                       Selling short their own company’s stock—however, they are permitted to sell short against
                        the box at year-end to lock in a gain (and defer taxation) as long as the position is closed
                        out within 20 days.
                       Taking short-swing profits—a short-swing profit is a profit realized, in a six-month period
                        or shorter, from trading their own company’s stock; any short-swing profit must be
                        disgorged (returned to the company).
                       Trading based on inside information (generally defined as nonpublic information which,
                        if known, could reasonably be expected to affect the market price of a security).

                      For purposes of insider trading, the definition of insider has been expanded to include anyone
                      who has access to material, nonpublic information.


                      Insider Rules
                      LO 7.r     Apply the insider trading regulations.

                      The Insider Trading and Securities Fraud Enforcement Act of 1988 (ITSFA) formally
                      expanded the definition of an insider and established penalties for the illegal use of nonpublic
                      information. Insiders may be held liable for more than just transactions in their own accounts.

                      The act recognizes the fiduciary responsibility of the insider to the issuer, to the stockholders,
                      and to others who might be affected by trades made with insider knowledge. Investors
                      who have suffered monetary damage because of insider trading have legal recourse against
                      the insider and against any other party who had control over the misuse of nonpublic
                      information.

                      Inside information is any information that has not been disseminated to, or is not readily
                      available to, the general public. To determine whether information is nonpublic, the SEC
                      considers the method by which information is released to the public and the timing of trades
                      relative to when the information is available for public evaluation. A tipper is one who gives
                      inside information, and a tippee is the recipient of inside information.




S79_LEM_U7.indd 147                                                                                                       24/05/22 11:02 PM
          148         Unit 7   The Registration Process



                                                     TAKE NOTE
                                                     Simply giving someone inside information, while imprudent, is not a violation of law.
                                                     When the information is used to trade for profit or to avoid a loss, a violation occurs. In
                                                     this event, both the tipper and tippee are liable.
                                        The key elements of insider liability under the insider trading rules follow.
                                         Does the tipper owe a fiduciary duty to the company, its stockholders, and so forth, and
                                          has this duty been compromised?
                                         Does the tipper meet the personal benefits test (even something as simple as enhancing a
                                          friendship or reputation)?
                                         Does the tippee know or should the tippee have known that the information was inside or
                                          confidential?
                                         Is the information material and nonpublic?
                                        Given these elements, a misstatement by a corporate insider could leave that person liable
                                        under the rules, and anyone who trades on that information could be liable as well.


                                        Written Supervisory Procedures
                                        As a result of the act, FINRA required member firms to establish written supervisory
                                        procedures designed to detect and prevent the misuse of inside information by persons
                                        affiliated with members. Most firms erect an information barrier to segregate the information
                                        flow between departments that may have sensitive information (e.g., investment banking) and
                                        the rest of the firm.


                                        Blue Sheets
                                        In initiating an investigation into possible insider trading, the SEC will send Blue Sheets to
                                        member firms that have customers who may be involved. If, for instance, the commission is
                                        investigating unusual trading patterns regarding ABCD common stock, the Blue Sheets will
                                        require members to provide the SEC with information on
                                         the identity of customers who bought or sold the common stock during a certain period,
                                         the number of shares bought or sold,
                                         the exact timing of these purchases or sales,
                                         the transactions in derivative securities such as options, and
                                         home addresses and phone numbers of the customers making these transactions.
                                        Failure to provide this information to the SEC on a timely basis will subject the member to
                                        serious disciplinary action.


                                        Civil Liability
                                        If the SEC determines that a person has violated the Insider Trading Act, it may seek a civil
                                        penalty against that person in a U.S. district court. The maximum penalty is three times
                                        profits made or losses avoided (treble damages). If that person is an employee of a member
                                        firm, the member firm (which is supposed to have procedures in place to prevent this) could
                                        be fined up to treble damages or $1 million, whichever is greater.

                                        Criminal penalties can be up to $5 million and 20 years in prison.




S79_LEM_U7.indd 148                                                                                                                                24/05/22 11:02 PM
                                                                                      Unit 7   The Registration Process   149


                                 TAKE NOTE
                                 Any fines levied for insider trading violations must be paid to the U.S. Treasury.


                      Statute of Limitations
                      A lawsuit can be brought in court by anyone who, at the same time, bought (if the insider
                      sold) or sold (if the insider bought) securities of the same class. The suit may be initiated up to
                      five years after the violation, and the damages imposed under a lawsuit of this kind can go up
                      to three times profits made or losses avoided by the insider.

                      The SEC, in its sole discretion, is permitted to pay bounties to informants in insider trading
                      cases. The total amount of the bounty that may be paid from a civil penalty cannot exceed
                      10% of that penalty.



                                                                                                                                 Unit 7
                      Regulation FD
                      Regulation Fair Disclosure (Regulation FD) was adopted by the SEC to curb the selective
                      disclosure of material nonpublic information by issuers to analysts and institutional investors.
                      Regulation FD requires that when an issuer discloses material information, it does so publicly
                      to provide a level playing field.

                      Regulation FD also addresses the intentional and unintentional disclosure of material,
                      nonpublic information. Whenever an issuer (or any person acting on its behalf )
                      discloses material, nonpublic information to any person outside the issuer, the issuer
                      must simultaneously make public disclosure of that same information if the disclosure is
                      intentional. In the case of unintentional disclosure, the issuer must promptly (before the start
                      of the next trading day) make public disclosure.

                      Regulation FD permits issuers to make public disclosure by filing or furnishing a Form 8-K
                      with the SEC or by disseminating information “through another method (or combination
                      of methods) of disclosure that is reasonably designed to provide broad, nonexclusionary
                      distribution of the information to the public.” Public conference calls, press releases, and
                      webcasts are allowable Regulation FD-compliant methods of public disclosure.


                      Regulation FD-Compliant Methods of Disclosure
                      Regulation FD-compliant methods of disclosure include any of the following or a
                      combination of the following:
                       A broadly disseminated press release
                       Filing a Form 8-K, 10-Q, or 10-K with the SEC
                       Widely available, nonexclusionary conference calls
                       Press conferences or webcasts with adequate notice that are freely accessible
                       Company websites
                      Companies must take care to bring disclosures to the attention of readers and not bury the
                      information or scatter it in a piecemeal fashion so as to cloud the issue.

                                 TAKE NOTE
                                 Company websites need special care. They cannot be for a privileged few. In order for
                                 information to be considered public, steps must be taken to make certain the website




S79_LEM_U7.indd 149                                                                                                       24/05/22 11:02 PM
          150         Unit 7   The Registration Process



                                                     is widely known and a recognized channel of distribution, and investors must be given
                                                     a reasonable waiting period to react to the information.


                                        Sarbanes-Oxley Act
                                        The Sarbanes-Oxley Act of 2002, sometimes referred to as SOX, was enacted in response
                                        to a number of major corporate and accounting scandals including Enron and WorldCom.
                                        This legislation established enhanced standards for all U.S. public company boards,
                                        management, and public accounting firms. Among other things, SOX created the Public
                                        Company Accounting Oversight Board to oversee, regulate, inspect, and discipline accounting
                                        firms in their roles as auditors of public companies. SOX also covers issues such as auditor
                                        independence, corporate governance, internal control assessment, and enhanced financial
                                        disclosure.
                                         Section 302 of SOX deals with internal control certifications. The signing officers must
                                          certify that they have reviewed all periodic financial reports, that they do not contain any
                                          material untrue statements or omissions of material facts, and that they have evaluated
                                          their internal controls within the previous 90 days and have reported on their findings.
                                         Section 401 of SOX deals with disclosure in periodic reports, focusing on the
                                          requirement that financial statements include all material off-balance sheet liabilities,
                                          obligations, or transactions.
                                         Section 402 of SOX requires enhanced conflict of interest provisions. The major result of
                                          this section is the prohibition of personal loans to executives.
                                         Section 403 of SOX requires that within 10 days of becoming an officer, director, or 10%
                                          shareholder of a publicly traded issuer, such person must file with the SEC a statement as
                                          to that person’s ownership of the common stock of the issuer.
                                         Section 404 of SOX requires issuers to publish information in their annual reports
                                          concerning the scope and adequacy of their internal accounting controls. Independent
                                          accounting firms, in the same report, must attest to and report on their assessment of the
                                          effectiveness of the issuer’s internal controls.

                      6.   A company is selling an additional issue of common stock. In this situation, all of the

                      Answers
                      1. C FINRA basically defines an insider as an officer, director or an individual owning
                           10% or more of the outstanding voting stock of a corporation. The definition
                           extends to the spouse of those individuals. LO 7.q

                      2. B Any change in insider holdings must be reported to the SEC within two business
                           days of the change. LO 7.q

                           issuer. The form is also filed with the issuer and with the market where the security
                           principally trades. LO 7.q

                      4. C The Securities Exchange Act of 1934 prohibits short-swing profits (profits realized
                           in any period less than six months) by corporate insiders in their own corporation’s
                           stock, except in very limited circumstance. If an insider does take a short-swing
                           profit, the profit is disgorged, or returned, to the company. This rule applies only
                           to directors or officers of the corporation and those holding greater than 10% of
                           the stock, and is designed to prevent insider trading by those most likely to have
                           access to important corporate information. LO 7.q

                           directors, and 10% or more beneficial owners. Additionally, 10-Ks and proxy filings
                           will include a list of officers, directors, and 5% shareholders. LO 7.r

                      6. D Under the Securities Exchange Act of 1934, an insider is defined as an officer,
                           director, or shareholder who owns 10% of more of the company’s outstanding
                           stock. The lead underwriter is not an insider, but may have access to inside
                           information. LO 7.r

             8
                                          Mergers and Acquisitions—
                                          Sell Side




          LEARNING OBJECTIVES
                      When you have completed this unit, you will be able to accomplish the following.
                      ff LO 8.a   Identify steps for setting up the sell side process.
                      ff LO 8.b   Determine due diligence factors through a transaction analysis.
                      ff LO 8.c   Use marketing activities to attract and retain buyers.
                      ff LO 8.d   Define a nonbinding indication of interest.
                      ff LO 8.e   Evaluate a buyer’s proposal.
                      ff LO 8.f   List the steps that lead to executing the transaction.


          INTRODUCTION
                      This unit covers the process companies take to either change ownership or alter their corporate
                      structure. These objectives are achieved through a merger or acquisition with another
                      company, a tender offer to purchase a large portion of a company’s shares, or the final option
                      for a distressed company, bankruptcy.

                      Mergers and acquisitions (M&A) is the process in which two companies are bought, sold, or
                      combined into one. While the lines between M&A are being blurred, a distinction can still
                      be made. Mergers are transactions where two companies combine (or merge) together to
                      create a new, separate company. The two original companies cease to exist in a merger, and the
                      shareholders of each company give up their shares in the original companies in exchange for
                      shares of the new company.

                      In an acquisition, one company purchases another company. Unlike a merger, no new
                      company is formed, and only one company ceases to exist. The selling company is bought
                      and is incorporated into the purchasing company. In this case, the shareholders of the
                      selling company are compensated through cash and/or securities, while shareholders of the
                      purchasing company are compensated through the expanded business from the purchase
                      (presumably increasing the value of the purchaser).
                                                                                                                        155



S79_LEM_5E.indb 155                                                                                               9/1/2020 1:55:31 PM
          156         Unit 8   Mergers and Acquisitions—Sell Side



                                        The process of entering and executing a merger or acquisition can be complicated, involving
                                        company valuation, marketing the deal, preparing regulatory filings, and closing the deal. As a
                                        result, investment bankers are frequently sought to assist in completing these transactions.


          LESSON 8.1: SETTING UP THE PROCESS
                                        LO 8.a      Identify steps for setting up the sell side process.



                                        The Process
                                        It can almost go without saying that the sale of a company or any of its components
                                        (including divisions, business lines, or major assets) is a large and difficult decision for the
                                        company’s management and board of directors. To aid them in this decision, an investment
                                        bank is often hired as an advisor to the process, occasionally referred to as a sell-side advisor.
                                        The investment banker’s responsibility will be to help the decision makers determine if, when,
                                        and how a sale should occur. This is not a simple process. The investment banker must be able
                                        to identify potential assets to sell, value the assets, and market the sale to ensure the company
                                        receives the best price in a reasonable time. Many of these issues will be industry specific and
                                        unique to the company itself.

                                        Before a company can begin to be marketed for sale, extensive preparations must be made
                                        by the investment banker to ensure as smooth of a transaction as possible. The investment
                                        banker must be formally engaged by the seller, identify the best type and structure of
                                        transaction, identify any potential tax or legal consequences of a transaction, and identify any
                                        impacts the corporate governance and contractual obligations may have on the transaction.
                                        A valuation analysis must also be conducted to aid in pricing the transaction. Lastly, the
                                        investment banker must conduct an extensive analysis on the potential buyers to identify who
                                        to solicit, determine the transaction’s marketability, and determine any necessary financing
                                        requirements.


                                        Engagement Letter
                                        The first step in any transaction is formally being engaged by the seller. To do this, the
                                        investment bank and the seller must both sign an engagement letter. This letter formally
                                        defines the relationship between the seller and the investment bank, and is often drafted by
                                        the legal counsels of both parties. While the content for each engagement letter may vary
                                        widely depending on the services to be rendered, the type of transaction, and the transaction’s
                                        jurisdiction, most components remain standard.

                                        The engagement letter should identify any parties involved (i.e., the seller and the investment
                                        bank), including any related third parties. The rights, duties, and responsibilities of each party
                                        during the transaction are defined. The scope of the transaction must likewise be identified.
                                        This is especially important for both parties, because any payment will be made based off
                                        the performance of a specific transaction. For example, an engagement letter may identify
                                        the transaction as being the divestiture of a specific division of the seller. Any activities the
                                        investment bank performs for the seller outside the scope of that specific divestiture would
                                        not be considered a part of the engagement and would not be subject to its terms.

                                        The part of the engagement letter most frequently debated will be the fee structure of the
                                        investment bank. Any investment bank has a reasonable expectation to be compensated
                                        for the services it renders. This compensation is generally made up of three parts: a deposit




S79_LEM_5E.indb 156                                                                                                                     9/1/2020 1:55:31 PM
                                                                          Unit 8   Mergers and Acquisitions—Sell Side   157


                      or retainer, out-of-pocket expenses incurred during the transaction (e.g., legal fees), and
                      performance-based fees (e.g., commissions). Each part of the fee must be disclosed and agreed
                      to in the engagement letter.

                                TAKE NOTE
                                While the engagement letter specifies the duties, responsibilities, and obligations
                                of both the investment bank and the seller, specific transactional information is not
                                included, such as the type or pricing of offering.

                                TAKE NOTE
                                Some expenses like due diligence are not out of pocket—they are substantial. In about
                                the last 10 years, FINRA has had a major crackdown on unreasonable due diligence
                                fees. When in doubt, disclose, disclose, disclose.


                      Transaction Analysis

                      LO 8.b    Determine due diligence factors through a transaction analysis.

                      Once an engagement letter has been signed, the investment banker will perform a due
                      diligence review on the seller. This due diligence generally starts with an in-depth meeting
                      with senior management to assess the objectives and needs of the seller. Understanding the
                      seller’s objectives and needs will allow the investment banker to present the seller with a
                      proposal of what transactions would be most appropriate (e.g., the sale of an entire company
                      or only divesting business segments). The investment banker will also need to address how                   Unit 8
                      the transaction will be structured. Transactions range from a simple sale of assets to seeking a
                      buyer to issue a tender offer.

                      When the investment banker is assessing the type and structure of the transaction,
                      consideration needs to be taken for any potential tax, antitrust, or other regulatory issues.


                      Transaction Type
                      One of the first questions that needs to be addressed by a seller’s investment banker is what
                      type of transaction would be best for the company? The choices are wide and varied, but some
                      of the more popular options are listed as follows.
                       Sale of entire company. The entire company could be sold in one whole unit.
                       Divestiture. In a divestiture, the company could be sold either partially or entirely.
                         Generally, business units would be sold off or liquidated. If the entire company is sold,
                         then it would be done in pieces. This is most suitable when the company is worth less
                         than the sum of its parts.
                       Spin-off. A spin-off is a type of divestiture, where a business unit or division is turned
                         into a subsidiary entity. Unlike a complete divestiture, in a spin-off, the parent company
                         could still control some or all of the spin-off.
                       Split-off. In a split-off, the parent company offers shareholders the option to keep their
                         current shares or exchange them for shares of the divesting company.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 157                                                                                                     9/1/2020 1:55:32 PM
          158         Unit 8   Mergers and Acquisitions—Sell Side



                                                    EXAMPLE
                                                    XYZ Company is a subsidiary of the ABC Corporation. In a split-off, ABC would organize
                                                    a new company, MNO, and transfer its ownership interest in XYZ to MNO. Shares of
                                                    MNO would then be offered to ABC shareholders, where the shareholders would use
                                                    their ABC shares to purchase a stake in MNO.


                                        Transaction Structure
                                        The structure of a merger or acquisition must also be investigated. Should it be a sale of the
                                        seller’s stock or the seller’s assets? Will it be for the entire or only a part of the company?
                                        Would a merger or a tender offer be preferred? The investment banker needs to consult with
                                        the seller about their objectives and interests. Doing so will allow the investment banker to
                                        make the best recommendations and assessments of potential transactions.


                                        Tax Consequences
                                        In the sale of any company, a major factor to consider when structuring the transaction is the
                                        tax implications to both the buyer and the seller. Depending on the structure, there could
                                        be significantly different tax burdens. An investment banker needs to assess all options and
                                        consult with the seller’s tax advisor as to which transaction would be best for the seller, and
                                        most conducive to effecting the sale.
                                         Tax-free reorganization. In a tax-free reorganization, the seller receives only stock as
                                           compensation. As a result, neither the buyer nor the seller will experience a gain or loss
                                           through the acquisition. The seller will, instead, defer any gains until the sale of the
                                           received stock.
                                         Recapitalization. Recapitalization is a change in the capital (debt and equity) structure
                                           of a company. A firm may, for example, issue additional equity to pay off debt holders,
                                           reducing the company’s leverage (and diluting shareholder’s worth). A firm may also wish
                                           to increase its debt to enjoy the tax advantage of its interest payments.
                                         IRS Rule 338(h)(10) Elections. In the case, a sale of a company is made using a
                                           338(h)(10) election; the buyer purchases the seller’s stock but recognizes it as a purchase
                                           of the seller’s assets. As a result, the seller would only have the tax obligation associated
                                           with a sale of assets rather than a sale of the stock.


                                        Antitrust and Regulatory Issues
                                        During many transactions, especially between a small number of buyers, there may be
                                        significant regulatory issues to consider. For example, certain mergers are required to make
                                        disclosure to the FTC under the Hart-Scott-Rodino Act, to prevent potential monopolies
                                        from forming. The HSR Act and its reporting requirements will be addressed in more detail
                                        shortly.

                                        When dealing in cross-border transactions there may be additional regulatory burdens,
                                        especially when dealing with publicly traded companies who may need to report to both
                                        foreign tax and securities authorities.




S79_LEM_5E.indb 158                                                                                                                          9/1/2020 1:55:33 PM
                                                                           Unit 8   Mergers and Acquisitions—Sell Side   159


                      Corporate Issues
                      A seller’s investment banker is also responsible for identifying any potential issues that may
                      arise simply from the corporate structure and environment of the company before entering
                      into any arrangement. These issues may include the following.
                       Golden parachutes. There may be costly packages in place for the executive team
                         that will make it prohibitively expensive to enter into an arrangement. While this will
                         incentivize the firm’s management to enter into a change in control of the company, IRS
                         Rule 280G causes any “excess parachute payments” to be penalized.
                       Plant closings. During the M&A process, the investment banker should also identify
                         potential changes in the firm’s operating facilities, specifically, which facilities should be
                         consolidated or closed to make the company more attractive to buyers.
                       Shareholder objectives. What are the objectives of the current shareholders? Are they
                         seeking dividend income or capital growth? When entering an M&A transaction, this will
                         have a large part to play in the type of firm to solicit and the structure of the transaction.
                       Corporate culture. While there may not be any standard metric indicating the effect of
                         corporate culture after a change in a company’s control, the effect is nonetheless present.
                         How employees will react to new standards and expectations can have a drastic impact on
                         worker productivity. The investment banks need to take this into account when advising a
                         seller as to how M&A should be approached.


                      Contractual Obligations
                      When restructuring a company, an investment banker will need to identify any implications

                                                                                                                                   Unit 8
                      on existing contractual obligations. For example, what will the effect be on any bond
                      covenants and leverage measures? How will the conversion feature for any convertible
                      securities be modified? Will consent be required from the firm’s stock or bondholders? These
                      items will ultimately need to be addressed by the seller’s management but should be identified
                      by the investment banker.


                      Financial Valuation of Seller
                      A large part of the investment banker’s due diligence on the company will be conducting a
                      financial valuation. This valuation will allow the investment banker to better know the needs
                      of the company and understand how to best market the transaction to potential buyers.

                      The investment banker will need to be familiar with and use a wide range of valuation
                      techniques discussed in an earlier unit to determine what the value of the company is. Of
                      special importance, however, will be assessing the current market condition by reviewing
                      similar recent transactions to determine what the market’s sentiment for the transaction will
                      be.

                                 TAKE NOTE
                                 When conducting due diligence, the investment banker will look at the seller from the
                                 eyes of a buyer and assess the company’s strengths and weaknesses to market the
                                 transaction better.
                      At the end of the due diligence process, the investment banker will provide the seller with a
                      comprehensive valuation report that will have a detailed analysis of
                       the impact the transaction will have on existing securities holders;




S79_LEM_5E.indb 159                                                                                                      9/1/2020 1:55:33 PM
          160         Unit 8   Mergers and Acquisitions—Sell Side



                                         the estimated sale price;
                                         the market conditions for the transaction; and
                                         the estimated time frame for major events such as receipt of bids and settlement.


                                        Potential Buyer Analysis
                                        The final step a seller’s investment banker must take before actively participating in an M&A
                                        transaction is identifying potential buyers. There are two types of buyers that would be
                                        interested in participating in an M&A transaction: strategic buyers and financial buyers.

                                        Strategic buyers are usually firms in the same industry and would be able to take advantage of
                                        the natural synergies between the two companies. Financial buyers are looking for companies
                                        that can be “flipped.” Financial buyers will purchase a company, attempt to make it more
                                        profitable or attractive to other potential buyers, and sell it as soon as possible.

                                                    TAKE NOTE
                                                    Financial buyers frequently engage in leveraged buyouts (LBOs), where the financing
                                                    to purchase the company comes from issuing debt.
                                                    When selecting potential buyers, a few considerations need to be taken:
                                                     Whether marketing should be wide-based or company specific
                                                     Financing requirements of buyers
                                                     Buyer’s capacity to pay
                                                     Market reaction to an M&A announcement


                                        Stapled Financing
                                        The seller may arrange for prepackaged financing for potential buyers. This prepackaged
                                        financing is known as stapled financing. By providing the stapled financing, it makes it easier
                                        for buyers to bid, and it serves as a benchmark for valuation of potential bidders and helps
                                        arrange competitive financing if they choose not to use the stapled financing. There is a
                                        conflict of interest inherent with stapled financing, however, because the investment bank is
                                        providing advice to the seller and potential financing to the buyer, possibly representing both
                                        sides of the transaction.

                      Answers
                      1. A A company typically hires an investment bank to sell itself or a material part of the
                           business. The sale of a company, division, business, or collection of assets (target)         Unit 8
                           is a major event for its owners (shareholders), management, employees, and other
                           stakeholders. It is an intense, time-consuming process with, high stakes, usually
                           spanning several months. Consequently, the seller typically hires an investment
                           bank and its team of trained professionals (sell-side advisor) to ensure that key
                           objectives are met and a favorable result is achieved. LO 8.a

                           productivity. While dividends, in general, speak to a culture of growth or income,

                           as a benchmark for valuation of potential bidders and helps arrange competitive
                           financing if they choose not to use the stapled financing. LO 8.b
